And as for the second: that the thing to which the judgment relates, such as a proposition or relation, is known in terms of conception, so it does not prevent direction to it, and is unknown in terms of judgment, but actually seeking its attainment is impossible. And this is contrary to conception, because what is unknown in terms of conception is absolutely unknown, since there is no knowledge before conception. The upshot is that the object of judgment can have knowledge attaching to it before judgment, unlike the object of conception. And it was answered that we choose that it is known in one respect, and we do not concede the impossibility of directing then to its unknown aspect; it is only impossible if the known aspect is not among its aspects and considerations in a way that removes it from being absolutely unknown. This is like when we know that we have something through which life and perceptions exist, and we seek it by its essence or by its distinguishing accidents from everything else, according to what is derived from definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm). So the unknown sought is not confined to the essence nor to the accident. And what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that the unknown is the essence and the known is some considerations is a verification of what is most important, namely the possibility of acquiring conception in terms of essence, and a reminder that the unknowability of the essence is necessary in what is sought to be conceived, so that even if something is sought by its essence, and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that is by proof (dalīl) not definition. And if one intends to acquire the accident itself, it is unknown in its essence. And what is mentioned in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that each of the unknown and known aspects occurs for a third thing that is the sought is an objection to the Imam based on his admission that what is known in summary is: known in one respect, unknown in one respect. And the two aspects are distinct: one is known without summary, and the other is not known at all, but when they come together in a thing, it is thought that there is summary knowledge. Otherwise, he himself mentioned in Naqd Tanzīl al-Afkār that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about the acquisition of conceptions (taṣawwurāt). It begins by stating Imam al-Razi's view that all acquired conceptions are necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning they cannot be acquired through voluntary acquisition. The reasoning is that acquisition requires the seeker to either already know the sought (impossible, as it is already had) or not know it (impossible to direct the mind to it). An objection is raised: the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, allowing direction and acquisition. A second objection uses the example of acquiring a judgment (taṣdīq), which is possible despite the proof being known. The response to the first objection argues that seeking from the known aspect is impossible (cannot acquire what is already known), and seeking from the unknown aspect is also impossible (cannot direct to the unknown). The response to the second objection distinguishes between conception and judgment: in judgment, the subject matter is known in conception, so direction is possible, but the judgment itself is unknown; this does not apply to conception, where if the thing is unknown in conception, it is absolutely unknown. A further response (from the objector's side) counters that the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, using the example of knowing we have a thing that gives life and perception, then seeking its essence or distinguishing accidents. The author then discusses works like al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal to clarify the nature of the unknown and known aspects, concluding that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity. The passage is dense and engages with multiple layers of objection and response.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
معلوم من وجه
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is known in any respect, it can be sought and acquired.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if partially known, seeking must occur from either the known or unknown aspect, both leading to impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is the acquisition of a judgment (taṣdīq) possible even though the proof is known?
A) Because judgment is not a conception and can be acquired through proof.
B) Because the subject of judgment is known in conception, allowing direction.
C) Because the proof itself is unknown.
D) Because judgment does not require direction.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's responses preserve his claim by distinguishing the partial knowledge of objects in judgment from the absolute unknowability of a conception that is unknown in conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a concept like 'the attributes of Allah,' do you often remain satisfied with a mere conception (tasawwur) without striving for a deeper judgment (tasdiq) that transforms your heart? What is one attribute you know of but have not truly affirmed in your actions?
?
Al-Razi argues that seeking something unknown is only possible if there is some prior knowledge. In your spiritual journey, have you tried to seek knowledge about faith without grounding it in the foundational knowledge of the Quran and Sunnah? How has that led to confusion or lack of conviction?
?
The object of judgment can have knowledge before the judgment itself. Is there a truth about your own shortcomings—like pride or procrastination—that you have conceptual knowledge of but have never actually judged as serious enough to change? What would it take for you to move from knowing about it to truly seeking to change it?

Refining the Objection: Known in One Respect
Refined objection, response, and analysis from later works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a further response refines the notion of 'known in one respect' using the example of definition and description, and how later works like al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal analyze the issue.

Core Idea
The known aspect must remove the object from being absolutely unknown for direction to be possible.

After al-Razi's responses, the objector presses further: if the sought is known in one respect, direction to its unknown aspect is possible. Al-Razi's defender counters that this only works if the known aspect removes the absolute unknowability. The example of seeking the essence of something known by its effects illustrates this. Later references to al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal clarify the subtle interplay between known and unknown aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the refined objection and response
Focus on the condition: the known aspect must remove absolute unknowability. The example of seeking a thing's essence by its effects is key.
This part introduces important terms: ḥadd (definition) and rasm (description).
2
Analyze the example
We know we have something that gives life and perceptions, and we seek it by its essence or distinguishing accidents. How does this work with the known/unknown distinction?
3
Examine the al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ references
These works clarify that the unknown is the essence, and the known is some considerations. The text explains that the unknown sought is the quiddity's essence.
This links to broader Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اختار الإمام الرازي أن كل ما يحصل من التصورات فهو ضروري لأن الاكتساب يمتنع من جهة المكتسب أعني المطلوب والكاسب أعني طريق اكتسابه. أما الأول : فلأن المطلوب إما أن يكون معلوماً فلا يمكن طلبه واكتسابه لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، أو يكون مجهولاً فلا يمكن التوجه إليه، ثم اعترض أحدهما : أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون معلوماً من وجه فيتوجه إليه مجهولاً من وجه فيطلب. وثانيهما: النقض باكتساب التصديق مع جريان الدليل فيه ـ فأجاب عن الأول : بأنه إما أن يطلب من وجهه المعلوم وهو محال لامتناع تحصيله، أو من وجهه المجهول وهو محال لامتناع التوجه إليه . وعن الثاني : بأن ما يتعلق به التصديق كالقضية أو النسبة معلوم بحسب التصور، فلا يمنع التوجه إليه، ومجهول بحسب التصديق يمتنع فعلا طلب حصوله . وهذا بخلاف التصور، فإن ما يكون مجهولاً بحسب التصور يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً، إذ لا علم قبل التصور، وحاصله أن متعلق التصديق يجوز أن يتعلق به قبل التصديق علم بخلاف متعلق التصور. وأجيب بأن تختار أنه معلوم من وجه ولا نسلم امتناع التوجه حينئذ إلى وجهه المجهول، وإنما يمتنع لو لم يكن الوجه المعلوم من وجوهه واعتباراته بحيث يخرجه عن كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، وذلك كما إذا علمنا أن لنا شيئاً به الحياة والإدراكات فتطلبه بحقيقته أو بعوارضه المميزة له عن جميع ما عداه، على ما هو المستفاد من الحد أو الرسم. فالمجهول المطلوب لا ينحصر في الحقيقة ولا في العارض وما ذكر في المواقف من أن المجهول هو الذات والمعلوم بعض الاعتبارات تحقيق لما هو الأهم، أعني إمكان اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة وتنبيه على أن مجهولية الذات لازمة فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو على الشيءء بحقيقته، وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا التعريف، ولو قصد اكتساب العارض نفسه كان هو مجهولاً بحقيقته، وما ذكر في تلخيص المحصل من أن كلا من الوجه المجهول والمعلوم حاصل لأمر ثالث هو المطلوب؛ إلزام للإمام بما اعترف به من أن المعلوم إجمالاً : معلوم من وجه . مجهول من وجه : والوجهان متغايران أحدهما معلوم لا إجمال فيه، والآخر ليس بمعلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء ظن أن هناك علماً إجمالياً. وإلا فقد ذكر هو في نقد تنزيل الأفكار : أن المطلوب المجهول هو حقيقة الماهية
I say: Imam al-Razi chose that every conception that occurs is necessary, because acquisition is impossible from the perspective of the acquired, namely the sought, and the acquirer, namely the way of its acquisition. As for the first: the sought is either known, so it cannot be sought and acquired due to the impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained, or it is unknown, so direction to it is impossible. Then one of them objected: why may it not be known in one respect and directed to as unknown in another respect and thus sought? And the second objection is the counterexample of acquiring a judgment (taṣdīq) while the proof is running. So he answered the first: that it must be sought either from its known aspect, which is impossible due to the impossibility of obtaining it, or from its unknown aspect, which is impossible due to the impossibility of directing to it. And as for the second: that the thing to which the judgment relates, such as a proposition or relation, is known in terms of conception, so it does not prevent direction to it, and is unknown in terms of judgment, but actually seeking its attainment is impossible. And this is contrary to conception, because what is unknown in terms of conception is absolutely unknown, since there is no knowledge before conception. The upshot is that the object of judgment can have knowledge attaching to it before judgment, unlike the object of conception. And it was answered that we choose that it is known in one respect, and we do not concede the impossibility of directing then to its unknown aspect; it is only impossible if the known aspect is not among its aspects and considerations in a way that removes it from being absolutely unknown. This is like when we know that we have something through which life and perceptions exist, and we seek it by its essence or by its distinguishing accidents from everything else, according to what is derived from definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm). So the unknown sought is not confined to the essence nor to the accident. And what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that the unknown is the essence and the known is some considerations is a verification of what is most important, namely the possibility of acquiring conception in terms of essence, and a reminder that the unknowability of the essence is necessary in what is sought to be conceived, so that even if something is sought by its essence, and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that is by proof (dalīl) not definition. And if one intends to acquire the accident itself, it is unknown in its essence. And what is mentioned in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that each of the unknown and known aspects occurs for a third thing that is the sought is an objection to the Imam based on his admission that what is known in summary is: known in one respect, unknown in one respect. And the two aspects are distinct: one is known without summary, and the other is not known at all, but when they come together in a thing, it is thought that there is summary knowledge. Otherwise, he himself mentioned in Naqd Tanzīl al-Afkār that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about the acquisition of conceptions (taṣawwurāt). It begins by stating Imam al-Razi's view that all acquired conceptions are necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning they cannot be acquired through voluntary acquisition. The reasoning is that acquisition requires the seeker to either already know the sought (impossible, as it is already had) or not know it (impossible to direct the mind to it). An objection is raised: the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, allowing direction and acquisition. A second objection uses the example of acquiring a judgment (taṣdīq), which is possible despite the proof being known. The response to the first objection argues that seeking from the known aspect is impossible (cannot acquire what is already known), and seeking from the unknown aspect is also impossible (cannot direct to the unknown). The response to the second objection distinguishes between conception and judgment: in judgment, the subject matter is known in conception, so direction is possible, but the judgment itself is unknown; this does not apply to conception, where if the thing is unknown in conception, it is absolutely unknown. A further response (from the objector's side) counters that the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, using the example of knowing we have a thing that gives life and perception, then seeking its essence or distinguishing accidents. The author then discusses works like al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal to clarify the nature of the unknown and known aspects, concluding that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity. The passage is dense and engages with multiple layers of objection and response.

The Six Types of Necessary Knowledge in Kalam
Enumeration of six types of necessary knowledge with their justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of necessary knowledge as classified in classical kalam and their role as foundational premises for theoretical reasoning.

Core Idea
The six types of necessary knowledge are the bedrock upon which all theological arguments rest.

In order to build sound theological arguments, scholars of kalam first needed to identify the kinds of knowledge that are immediately certain. This passage by Sanusi enumerates six such types, explaining how each is justified. As you read, notice how each type relies on a distinct source of certainty — from pure reason to sense perception to widespread testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two parts: the enumeration of six types and then an explanation of why this matters for kalam.
2
Identify each type and its justification
For each of the six categories, note the specific way the intellect makes its judgment (e.g., by mere conceptualization, by external sense, etc.).
Try to memorize the Arabic terms; they are technical vocabulary in kalam.
3
Understand the methodological role
After the enumeration, Sanusi explains why these types are discussed in kalam: because theoretical knowledge reduces to necessary knowledge. Reflect on why proving necessary truths is foundational.
This connects the list to the broader purpose of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: العلوم الضرورية تنحصر في ست بديهيات، يحكم العقل بها بمجرد تصور الطرفين وتسمى الأوليات (۱) ومشاهدات يحكم بها بواسطة حس ظاهر وتسمى الحسيات أو باطن وتسمى الوجدانيات، وفطريات ويحكم بها بواسطة لا تغرب عن الذهن وتسمى قضايا قياساتها ومجربات يحكم بها بواسطة تكرر المشاهدة ومتواترات يحكم بها بمجرد خبر جماعة يمتنع تواطؤها على الكذب، وحدسيات يحكم بها بواسطة حدس من (٢) النفس وستعرفه) . أقول: لما كانت العلوم النظرية تنتهي إلى الضروريات جعلوا إثباتها (۳) والرد على منكريها من مبادىء الكلام ليعلم أن ما يجعل منتهى مقدمات القياس ويدعي كونه ضرورياً هل هو منها ، ولم يشتغلوا بضبط التصورات الضرورية وكأنها ترجع إلى البديهات والمشاهدات وحصروا التصديقات
The third section: The necessary sciences are confined to six self-evidents (badihiyyat), upon which the intellect judges by mere conceptualization of the two extremes, and they are called primary intellects (awwaliyyat) (1); and observations (mushahadat), judged by means of an external sense, called sensibles (hissiyyat) or internal sense, called found things (wijdaniyyat); and innate things (fitriyyat), judged by means of what does not escape the mind, called propositions whose syllogisms (qadaya qiyasatuha); and experiential things (mujarrabat), judged by means of repeated observation; and successively reported things (mutawatirat), judged by mere report of a group whose collusion on falsehood is impossible; and intuitive things (hadisiyyat), judged by means of an intuition from the soul, and you will know it. I say: Since the theoretical sciences end in necessary things, they (the scholars) made proving them and refuting their deniers among the principles of kalam, so that it may be known whether that which is made the final point of the premises of the syllogism and is claimed to be necessary is actually among them. And they did not occupy themselves with defining necessary concepts, as if they refer to self-evidents and observations, and they confined the assertions.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage classifies necessary knowledge into six types. The author lists them: (1) awwaliyyat (primary intellects) – self-evident propositions whose judgment requires only understanding the terms; (2) mushahadat (observations) – based on external senses (hissiyyat) or internal senses (wijdaniyyat); (3) fitriyyat (innate) – judged by a natural disposition that does not leave the mind, also called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them'; (4) mujarrabat (experiential) – derived from repeated observation; (5) mutawatirat (successive reports) – based on a large group whose agreement on falsehood is impossible; (6) hadisiyyat (intuitive) – based on an intuition from the soul. He then gives a reason (أقول) for discussing them in kalam: theoretical sciences reduce to necessary truths, so proving the necessary truths and refuting those who deny them is among the principles of kalam. This is to verify whether a claimed necessary premise is truly necessary. They did not bother to define necessary concepts precisely, as they rely on self-evidents and observations, and they limited the assertions (perhaps meaning they only considered basic assertions).

Key Terms

الوجدانيات
الفطريات
المجربات
المتواترات
الحدسيات
التصورات الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these six categories are mutually exclusive or exhaustive in all contexts.
Correction: The passage presents them as standard types in kalam, but in practice some knowledge may fall under multiple categories or other classifications may exist.

Checkpoint
Which type of necessary knowledge is judged by mere report of a group whose collusion on falsehood is impossible?
A) Awwaliyyat
B) Mushahadat
C) Mutawatirat
D) Hadisiyyat

Key Takeaway
The six types of necessary knowledge — awwaliyyat, mushahadat, fitriyyat, mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and hadisiyyat — are the foundational certainties that underpin all theoretical reasoning in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a doubt about a core Islamic belief, can you trace that doubt back to a missing or weakened necessary premise? For example, do you truly accept, like a primary intellect, that the Creator must be one? Or have you allowed dubious premises to creep in?
?
The scholars of kalam made these necessary truths the foundation of their arguments to guard against skepticism. Do you feel that your own conviction in the existence of Allah and the truth of Islam rests on such solid, necessary grounds? If not, what would it take for you to build your faith on premises as certain as 'fire burns'?

Definition of 'Awwaliyyat' (First Principles)
Definition of 'awwaliyyat' as first principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'awwaliyyat' (first principles) and why they are considered self-evident foundations of knowledge.

Core Idea
First principles are self-evident truths that the intellect grasps without proof.

This lesson introduces the concept of 'awwaliyyat'—the foundational premises that are immediately certain without needing demonstration. These are the building blocks of all rational knowledge, and understanding them is crucial for kalam. The passage defines them and provides an example from Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the multiple names given to this category.
2
Identify the key characteristics
From the definition, list the essential features of awwaliyyat: they are certain, necessary, do not require a middle term, and are self-justifying.
Think about what 'no middle term' means.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider Ibn Sina's example: 'The whole is greater than the part.' Why is this proposition known without sensory experience or induction?
This illustrates the self-evident nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) الأوليات : هي المقدمات اليقينية الضرورية، وتسمى بالمبادىء الأولى والبديهيات ومبادىء المنطق، ومباديء العقل، وهي ما لا يحتاج العقل في معرفته إلى وسط . قال ابن سينا : الأوليات قضايا ومقدمات تحدث في الإنسان من جهة قوته العقلية من غير سبب يوجب التصديق بها إلا ذواتها. ومثال ذلك : أن الكل أعظم من الجزء وهذا غير مستفاد من حس ولا استقراء ولا شيء آخر، وأما التصديق بهذه القضية فهو من جملة الإنسان. (راجع النجاة ص .(۱۰
(1) The primary intellects (awwaliyyat): they are certain necessary premises, and they are called first principles, self-evidents, principles of logic, and principles of intellect. They are that which the intellect does not need a middle term to know. Ibn Sina said: The awwaliyyat are propositions and premises that occur in a human from his rational power without any cause necessitating belief in them except themselves. An example of that is: The whole is greater than the part. And this is not derived from sense, nor from induction, nor from anything else. As for belief in this proposition, it is from the very nature of the human. (Cf. al-Najat, p. 10).
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines 'awwaliyyat' (primary intellects), the first category of necessary knowledge. They are defined as certain, necessary premises that do not require a middle term for their cognition. They have several names: first principles, self-evidents, principles of logic, principles of intellect. The author quotes Ibn Sina to elaborate: awwaliyyat are propositions that arise from the rational faculty without any external cause compelling belief; rather, the proposition itself compels belief. The example given is 'the whole is greater than the part', which is not derived from sense experience or induction. The belief is inherent to human nature. The passage also references al-Najat for further reading.

المقدمات اليقينية
البديهيات
مبادىء العقل
وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all knowledge comes from sensory experience or induction.
Correction: The passage shows that first principles like 'the whole is greater than the part' are not derived from sense or induction; they are self-evident and known innately by the intellect.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change in your life if you truly acted on the self-evident truths of faith—like Allah's existence and oneness—as firmly as you act on mathematical axioms?
?
Ibn Sina says belief in first principles occurs 'without any cause necessitating belief except themselves.' Have you ever experienced that kind of immediate certainty about a spiritual truth? If not, why might your intellect be blocked from seeing what is inherently obvious? What habit or assumption is preventing you from seeing the signs of Allah as self-evident?
?
The passage teaches that some truths are known without a middle term—they are directly grasped by the intellect. In your daily life, you rely on this capability constantly. Are you aware of the moments when you use it? Consider one self-evident moral truth that you know is true without anyone teaching it to you. Are you living by it, or are you suppressing it? What would it cost you to fully obey that truth starting today?

Classification of Necessary Propositions
Taftazani classifies necessary propositions by judgment conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessary propositions are classified based on whether the intellect needs external aid to judge.

Core Idea
Necessary propositions either need external aid or they don't.

In this passage, Taftazani lays out a logical classification of necessary propositions. He starts with a binary question: does the intellect need help to judge? The answer determines whether a proposition is an axiom (self-evident) or depends on some external factor. This classification reveals the richness of what we consider 'necessary' knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification question
Begin by reading the opening question: is the conception of the extremes sufficient for the intellect's judgment? Notice the conditions (attention, soundness of instruments).
This sets up the entire classification.
2
Identify the two main branches
If conception alone suffices, it's axioms. If not, the intellect needs external aid. Then external aid is divided into three kinds: aid to the intellect, aid to the proposition, or both.
3
Trace each sub-branch
Under each kind of aid, Taftazani names specific types: observations, innates, intuitives, theoreticals, recurrent reports, and experiential. Note how each is defined by the nature of the aid.
This is a tree structure; understanding the logic helps memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن القضايا إما أن يكون تصور أطرافها بعد شرائط الإدراك من الالتفات وسلامة الالآت كافياً في حكم العقل أو لا؟ فإن كان كافياً فهي البديهيات، وإن لم يكن كافياً فلا محالة يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إلى العقل ويعينه على الحكم، أو إلى القضية أو إليهما جميعاً. فالأول: المشاهدات لاحتياجها إلى الإحساس. والثاني: لا يخلو من أن يكون ذلك الأمر لازماً وهي الفطريات أو غير لازم، وحينئذ إن كان حصوله بسهولة فهي الحدسيات وإلا فليست من الضروريات بل من النظريات. والثالث: إن كان حصوله بالأخبار فالمتواترات وإلا فالمجربات. أما البديهيات وتسمى أوليات فهي قضايا يحكم العقل بها بمجرد تصور طرفيها كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، والجسم الواحد لا يكون في آن واحد في مكانين، وقد يتوقف فيه العقل لعدم تصور الطرفين، كما في قولنا: الأشياء المتساوية لشيء واحد متساوية، أو لنقصان الغريزة كما في الصبيان والبله، أو لتدنيس الفطرة بالعقائد المضادة كما في بعض الجهال، أو لأن الله تعالى لا يخلقه على ما هو المذهب. وأما المشاهدات فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحواس الظاهرة.
Because propositions: Either the conception of their extremes after the conditions of perception, such as attention and soundness of instruments, is sufficient for the intellect's judgment or not? If sufficient, they are axioms. If not sufficient, then inevitably it needs something that joins the intellect and helps it to judge, or to the proposition, or to both. The first: observations, due to their need for sense. The second: it is not free from that thing being necessary, and they are the innates, or not necessary; then if its attainment is easy, they are intuitive; otherwise they are not from the necessary but from the theoretical. The third: if its attainment is through reports, then recurrent reports; otherwise experiential. As for axioms, also called primary truths, they are propositions that the intellect judges by mere conception of their two extremes, such as the judgment that one is half of two, and the single body cannot be in two places at one time. The intellect may be hindered [from judging] due to non-conception of the two extremes, as in our saying: things equal to one thing are equal, or due to deficiency of instinct as in children and the mentally deficient, or due to defilement of the innate disposition by contrary beliefs as in some ignorant people, or because God Most High does not create it according to the [adopted] doctrine. And as for observations, they are propositions that the intellect judges by means of the external senses.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a logical classification and definition. It begins with a condition: a proposition is either judged by mere conception (axioms) or needs external aid. If external aid is needed, it can be: (1) sense perception (observations), (2) a necessary aid (innates) or a non-necessary aid that is easy (intuitive) or difficult (theoretical, not necessary), (3) aid from reports (recurrent) or repeated observation (experiential). Then axioms (badihiyyat) are defined in detail: judgments by mere conception, with examples. Hindrances to such judgments include: lack of conception (e.g., 'things equal to one thing equal'), defective instinct, corrupting beliefs, or God withholding creation of the judgment (a theological point). Observations are defined as judgments via external senses.

Correction: Many necessary propositions require sense perception, innate knowledge, intuition, reports, or repeated experience.

Necessary propositions are classified based on whether the intellect can judge them alone or needs external help from senses, innate knowledge, intuition, reports, or repeated experience.

Axioms: Definition and Hindrances
Taftazani defines axioms and lists hindrances to their judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what axioms (badihiyyat) are and why the intellect may fail to judge them despite their self-evidence.

Core Idea
Axioms are self-evident truths the intellect judges by mere conception.

After classifying propositions, Taftazani now delves into the first category: axioms. He defines them as propositions the intellect judges without any external aid, merely by understanding the terms. However, he also notes several obstacles that can prevent a person from recognizing an axiom as self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of axioms
Read the Arabic text: 'أما البديهيات وتسمى أوليات فهي قضايا يحكم العقل بها بمجرد تصور طرفيها'. Note that axioms are also called 'primary truths' (awwaliyyat).
The examples given: 'one is half of two' and 'a body cannot be in two places at once'.
2
Identify the four hindrances
The text lists four reasons why someone might not judge an axiom: lack of conception, deficient instinct, corrupted disposition, or divine withholding. Read each and think of examples.
The first hindrance is exemplified by the proposition 'things equal to one thing are equal' – some may not conceive it.
3
Reflect on the theological point
The fourth hindrance is that God may not create the judgment in the intellect. This reflects the Ash'ari doctrine that judgments are created by God directly.
This highlights the theological underpinnings of epistemology in kalam.

Observations: Knowledge Through the Senses
Taftazani defines observations as judgments via external senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observations (mushahadat) provide necessary knowledge through the external senses.

Core Idea
Observations are propositions the intellect judges with the help of sense perception.

Taftazani now turns to the second category of necessary propositions: observations. These are judgments that cannot be made by reason alone but require input from the external senses. This category includes empirical knowledge gained through sight, hearing, etc. Observations are still necessary because sense perception, when conditions are sound, yields certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of observations
Read the Arabic: 'وأما المشاهدات فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحواس الظاهرة'. Note the phrase 'by means of the external senses'.
Contrast with axioms: observations need sense input, axioms do not.
2
Consider examples
Think of propositions like 'The sun is shining' or 'This object is hot'. These require sense perception but are still necessary truths when perceived correctly.
The necessity comes from the reliability of the senses under proper conditions.
3
Relate to the classification tree
Recall that in the tree, observations fall under the first branch of external aid: aid to the intellect via sense perception.
This connects back to Lesson 1.

The Six Types of Necessary Knowledge
Classification of necessary propositions into six types based on epistemic aid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of necessary propositions in Islamic logic and how they are distinguished based on whether the intellect requires external aid to judge.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge comes in six forms, each with its own epistemic basis.

In Islamic epistemology, necessary propositions (al-ḍarūriyyāt) form the foundation of certain knowledge. But not all necessary knowledge is immediate and self-evident. In this passage, the author classifies them into six distinct types based on what aids the intellect in reaching its judgment. Let's explore this fundamental taxonomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the six types
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the six terms: al-badīhiyyāt, al-mushāhadāt, al-fiṭriyyāt, al-mujarrabāt, al-mutawātirāt, al-ḥadasiyyāt.
The list is given at the very beginning.
2
Understand the main criterion
The author uses a key question: is mere conception of the terms sufficient for the intellect to judge? If yes, it's badīhiyyāt. If not, something else is needed to aid the intellect.
This criterion separates self-evident truths from others.
3
Trace the branches of the classification
Follow the author's division: if not self-evident, the additional aid can come from sense perception (mushāhadāt), a necessary concomitant (fiṭriyyāt), or easy attainment (ḥadasiyyāt) – else it becomes theoretical. Separately, aid can come from repeated reports (mutawātirāt) or repeated experience (mujarrabāt).
The classification covers both immediate and mediate necessary knowledge.
4
Reflect on the significance
This taxonomy helps us understand the sources of certainty in Islamic thought, from direct observation to consensus via tawātur. These categories are used in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرورية في ست البديهيات والمشاهدات والفطريات والمجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات لأن القضايا إما أن يكون تصور أطرافها بعد شرائط الإدراك من الالتفات وسلامة الآلات كافياً في حكم العقل أو لا فإن كان كافياً فهي البديهيات وإن لم يكن كافياً فلا محالة يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إلى العقل ويعينه على الحكم أو إلى القضية أو إليهما جميعاً فالأول المشاهدات لاحتياجها إلى الإحساس والثاني لا يخلو من أن يكون ذلك الأمر لازماً وهي الفطريات أو غير لازم وحينئذ إن كان حصوله بسهولة فهي الحدسيات وإلا فليست من الضروريات بل من النظريات والثالث إن كان حصوله بالأخبار فالمتواترات وإلا فالمجربات
The necessary [propositions] are six: al-badihiyyat (self-evident), al-mushahadat (observational), al-fitriyyat (innate), al-mujarrabat (experiential), al-mutawatirat (recurrently reported), and al-hadsiyyat (intuitive). Because propositions are either such that the conception of their terms after the conditions of perception – attention and soundness of the instruments – is sufficient for the judgment of the intellect, or not. If it is sufficient, then they are al-badihiyyat. If it is not sufficient, then inevitably it needs something to join the intellect and aid it in judgment, either for the proposition or for both of them together. The first [of these] is al-mushahadat because they need sense perception. The second: that thing is either necessary – and they are al-fitriyyat – or not necessary; then if its attainment is easy, they are al-hadsiyyat; otherwise they are not from the necessary but from the theoretical. The third: if its attainment is through reports, then [they are] al-mutawatirat; otherwise [they are] al-mujarrabat.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage classifies necessary knowledge (al-daruriyyat) into six types. The author first lists them: badihiyyat, mushahadat, fitriyyat, mujarrabat, mutawatirat, hadsiyyat. Then he provides a logical division: he considers whether mere conception of the terms, given proper attention and sound faculties, is sufficient for the intellect to judge. If yes, that is badihiyyat. If not, something must be added: either sense perception (mushahadat), or a necessary concomitant (fitriyyat), or an easy concurrence (hadsiyyat) – else it is theoretical. Alternatively, the aid may come from repeated reports (mutawatirat) or repeated experience (mujarrabat). This classification shows the epistemic basis of necessary propositions.

Key Terms
الضروريات
البديهيات
المشاهدات
الفطريات
المجربات
المتواترات
الحدسيات
النظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary knowledge is self-evident and requires no external aid.
Correction: Only al-badīhiyyāt are self-evident; other types like mushāhadāt, fiṭriyyāt, etc. depend on sense perception, innate concomitants, or repeated experience/reports.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, if mere conception of terms is sufficient for judgment, the proposition is:
A) Mashhurat
B) Badihiyyat
C) Mutawatirat
D) Mujarrabat

Key Takeaway
The six types of necessary knowledge—al-badīhiyyāt, al-mushāhadāt, al-fiṭriyyāt, al-mujarrabāt, al-mutawātirāt, al-ḥadasiyyāt—cover all propositions known with certainty through immediate conception or with the aid of sense, innate intuition, experience, or recurrent reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these six types of necessary knowledge grounds your certainty in the existence of Allah? Have you ever traced your belief back to its epistemic source, or do you take it for granted?
?
Al-mushahadat (observational knowledge) relies on sense perception. How often do you let your physical senses—what you see and hear—shape your religious convictions? If your senses can be mistaken, what does that mean for the certainty you derive from them?
?
Al-mutawatirat (recurrently reported knowledge) is foundational for transmitted truths like the Qur'an and Sunnah. When was the last time you actively reflected on the chain of transmission that brought you the words of revelation? Are you living as if you truly know their certainty, or as if they are mere stories?

Self-Evident Propositions (Badihiyyat)
Definition of badihiyyat with examples and conditions for hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of badihiyyat (self-evident propositions), their role as the foundation of knowledge, and the reasons the intellect may hesitate to affirm them.

Core Idea
Self-evident propositions are the bedrock of all knowledge, affirmed without proof.

Sanusi begins by laying the epistemological foundation: some propositions are so clear that the mind accepts them immediately, without any reasoning. These are called badihiyyat or awwaliyyat. Understanding them is crucial because all theoretical knowledge ultimately rests on them. This passage defines them, gives examples, and explains why even self-evident truths can sometimes be doubted.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of badihiyyat
Read the first few lines carefully. Note that they are 'primary propositions true in themselves' and affirmed without demonstration. The example given is 'the whole is greater than the part'.
This is a classical example used in logic.
2
Distinguish between badihi and nazari
The passage divides all knowledge into two kinds: self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). Make sure you understand the difference – the former requires no reflection, the latter does.
3
Examine the reasons for hesitation
The text lists four reasons why someone might not affirm a self-evident proposition: failure to conceive the terms, deficient instinct, corrupt beliefs, or divine non-creation. Reflect on each.
This shows that self-evidence is relative to the knower's circumstances.
4
Connect to the commentary
Read the commentary to see how these concepts fit into the larger framework of Islamic logic and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البديهيات : قضايا أولية صادقة بذاتها يجزم بها العقل من دون برهان ومفردها (بديهية) مثال ذلك : الكل أعظم من الجزء والأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية. وقد سميت بالبديهيات لأن الذهن يلحق محمول القضية بموضوعها من دون توسط شيء آخر وهي أساس العلم، لأن العلم إما بديهي، وهو الذي لا يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور الحرارة والبرودة، وكالتصديق بأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يفترقان وإما النظري وهو الذي يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور المعاني العلمية والتصديق بقوانين الطبيعة . وأما البديهيات وتسمى أوليات فهي قضايا يحكم العقل بها بمجرد تصور طرفيها كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، والجسم الواحد لا يكون في آن واحد في مكانين، وقد يتوقف فيه العقل لعدم تصور الطرفين، كما في قولنا : الأشياء المتساوية لشيء واحد متساوية، أو لنقصان الغريزة كما في الصبيان والبله، أو لتدنيس الفطرة بالعقائد المضادة كما في بعض الجهال، أو لأن الله تعالى لا يخلقه على ما هو المذهب. وأما المشاهدات فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحواس الظاهرة وتسمى حسيات كالحكم بأن الشمس نيرة والنار حارة، أو الباطنة وتسمى وجدانيات كالحكم بأن لنا خوفاً وغضباً، ومنها ما نجده بنفوسنا لا بالآلات البدنية كشعورنا بذواتنا وأحوالها، وجميع أحكام الحس جزئية لأنه لا يفيد إلا أن هذه النار حارة . وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة فحكم عقلي، حصل بمعونة الإحساس بجزئيات ذلك الحكم والوقوف على علله . الحسيات جمع الحس، وتسمى المحسوسات أيضاً، وتطلق في القضايا على معنيين الأول: هو القضايا التي يجزم بها العقل بمجرد تصور طرفيها بواسطة الحس الظاهر أو الباطن. وهي كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة من المشاهدات فإذا كانت الحس الظاهر سمیت محسوسات مثل حكمنا بوجود الشمس وإفادتها ووجود النار وحرارتها ووجود الثلج وبياضه . وإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الباطن سميت وجدانيات مثل شعورنا بأن لنا فكرة وإرادة وخوفاً وغضباً .
Al-badihiyyat: primary propositions that are true in themselves, which the intellect affirms without demonstration. Their singular is 'badihiyya'. Example: the whole is greater than the part, and things equal to one thing are equal. They are called badihiyyat because the mind attaches the predicate of the proposition to its subject without any intermediary. They are the foundation of knowledge, because knowledge is either badihi (self-evident) – which does not depend on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of heat and cold, and the affirmation that negation and affirmation cannot coexist nor both be absent – or nazari (theoretical) – which depends on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of scientific meanings and the affirmation of natural laws. As for al-badihiyyat, also called awwaliyyat, they are propositions which the intellect judges by mere conception of their two terms, such as the judgment that one is half of two, and that one body cannot be in two places at one time. The intellect may hesitate regarding them due to failure to conceive the two terms, as in our saying: things equal to one thing are equal, or due to deficiency of instinct as in children and the feeble-minded, or due to contamination of the innate disposition by contrary beliefs as in some ignoramuses, or because Allah – exalted – does not create it, according to the doctrine. As for al-mushahadat (observational propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges by means of the external senses. They are called hissiyyat (sensibles), like the judgment that the sun is bright and fire is hot; or by the internal senses, called wijdaniyyat (introspective), like the judgment that we have fear and anger. Among them are things we find in our souls not through bodily organs, like our awareness of ourselves and our states. All sense judgments are particular because they only entail that this fire is hot. As for the judgment that every fire is hot, it is an intellectual judgment obtained with the aid of sense perception of particulars of that judgment and knowledge of its causes. Al-hissiyyat is the plural of al-hiss. It is also called al-mahsusat. It is applied in propositions to two meanings: first, propositions which the intellect affirms by mere conception of their two terms by means of the external or internal senses. They are all particular judgments obtained from observations. So if they are by external sense, they are called mahsusat, like our judgment that the sun exists and gives light, that fire exists and is hot, that snow exists and is white. If they are by internal sense, they are called wijdaniyyat, like our feeling that we have thought, will, fear, and anger.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines two types of necessary propositions: al-badihiyyat (self-evident) and al-mushahadat (observational). The commentary begins by giving a definition of badihiyyat as primary propositions that are true in themselves, affirmed without demonstration. They are foundational to knowledge, and knowledge is divided into self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). Then the main text defines badihiyyat (also called awwaliyyat) as those judged by mere conception of terms, with examples. It notes that the intellect may hesitate due to failure to conceive terms, deficient instinct, corrupt beliefs, or divine non-creation. Then it defines mushahadat as propositions judged by means of the senses, either external (hissiyyat) or internal (wijdaniyyat). It emphasizes that sense judgments are particular; universal judgments like "every fire is hot" are intellectual, aided by sense. The footnote explains hissiyyat further, dividing them into external (mahsusat) and internal (wijdaniyyat). This provides a clear distinction between immediate self-evidence and sense-based observation.

الأوليات
البديهي
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident propositions are always accepted by everyone without exception.
Correction: The intellect may hesitate due to failure to conceive terms, deficient instinct, corrupt beliefs, or divine non-creation.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT a reason for hesitation regarding a badihiyya proposition?
A) Failure to conceive the two terms
B) Deficiency of instinct
C) Lack of sensory experience
D) Contamination of innate disposition by contrary beliefs

Key Takeaway
Badihiyyat are foundational propositions affirmed without demonstration, but their acceptance can be hindered by cognitive or external obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there any self-evident realities about Allah that you have hesitated to accept because of intellectual pride or spiritual laziness? What step will you take today to remove that obstacle?
?
The passage says that even self-evident propositions may not be accepted if Allah does not create the judgment. How does that make you rely on Allah for even the most basic knowledge? In what area of your life do you need to ask Allah to grant you clear perception?

Observational Propositions (Mushahadat)
Definition of mushahadat and their subdivision into external and internal senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mushahadat (observational propositions), their division into external and internal senses, and the key fact that their judgments are particular, not universal.

Core Idea
Observational propositions depend on the senses and yield only particular judgments.

After establishing self-evident propositions, Sanusi turns to another source of immediate knowledge: observation. These propositions come through the senses, either external (like sight) or internal (like introspection). The passage clarifies that sense judgments are always about particular instances, while universal judgments require intellectual reasoning. This distinction is vital for understanding how empirical knowledge fits into the broader framework of certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mushahadat
Read the opening sentences. Note that mushahadat are propositions judged by means of the senses, and they are divided into hissiyyat (external) and wijdaniyyat (internal).
Examples: 'the sun is bright' (external), 'we have fear' (internal).
2
Understand particularity of sense judgments
The text emphasizes that sense judgments are particular, not universal. For example, we sense that 'this fire is hot', but the statement 'every fire is hot' is a universal judgment reached by the intellect with the aid of sense.
This is a key epistemological point.
3
Examine the two meanings of hissiyyat
The footnote (integrated in the text) gives two meanings: (1) propositions affirmed by mere conception of terms via external or internal senses, and (2) particular judgments from observations. Focus on the first meaning as it relates to the definition.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how the particularity of sense judgments affects the certainty of empirical knowledge. The text shows that sense data alone does not give universal knowledge without intellectual processing.

?

Innate Propositions (Fitriyyat) – Knowledge Built into Conception
Sanusi defines fitriyyat and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what fitriyyat are, how the intellect judges them using a necessary concomitant, and why they are called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them.'

Core Idea
Some judgments come with their own built-in syllogism.

The first category of necessary propositions is innate to the rational mind. When you conceive the subject and predicate, a necessary relation becomes evident without external proof. Sanusi illustrates this with the example of four being even.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage for fitriyyat and its translation. Notice the phrase 'qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha' – propositions whose syllogisms are with them.
2
Identify the necessary concomitant
In the example 'four is even' the concomitant is divisibility into equal halves. Understand that this is not an external proof but inherent.
This distinguishes fitriyyat from acquired knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفطريات فقضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة لا يعزب عنه عند تصور الطرف وهو المعنى بأمر لازم منضم إلى القضية، ولهذا تسمى قضايا قياساتها معها، كالحكم بأن الأربعة زوج، لانقسامها بمتساويين . وأما المجربات : فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بانضمام تكرر المشاهدة إليه ، والقياس الخفي المنتج لليقين إليها أن الوقوع المتكرر على نهج واحد لا بد له من سبب وإن لم يعرف ماهيته فكلما علم وجود السبب علم وجود المسبب قطعاً، وذلك كالحكم بأن السقمونيا مسهل للصفراء . وأما المتواترات فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة كثرة شهادة المخبرين . المجربات : أمور أوقع التصديق بها الحس بشركة القياس، وذلك أنه إذا تكرر في إحساسنا وجود شيء لشيء مثل الاسهال للسقموينا والحركات المرصودة للسماويات تكرر ذلك منا في الذكر. وإذا تكرر منا ذلك في الذكر، حدثت لنا منه تجربة بسبب اقتران بالذكر، وهو أنه لو كان هذا الأمر كالاسهال مثلاً عن السقمونيا اتفاقياً عرضياً لا عن مقتضى طبيعته لكان لا يكون في أكثر الأمر من غير اختلاف، حتى انه إذا لم يوجد ذلك استندت النفس الواقعة فطلبت سبباً لما عرض من أنه لم يوجد فالمجربات : قضايا وأحكام تتبع مشاهدات منا تتكرر.
As for al-fitriyyat (innate propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges by means of something that does not escape it when conceiving the term, meaning a necessary concomitant attached to the proposition. For this reason they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them, like the judgment that four is even because it is divisible into two equal parts. As for al-mujarrabat (experiential propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges with the addition of repeated observation to it, and the hidden syllogism that produces certainty to them: that repeated occurrence in one manner must have a cause even if its nature is not known; so whenever the cause is known, the effect is known with certainty. For example, the judgment that scammony is a purgative for bile. As for al-mutawatirat (recurrently reported propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges by means of the multitude of reporters. Al-mujarrabat: Things whose affirmation is effected by sense with the participation of syllogism. That is, when in our sensation the existence of something for something is repeated, like purging for scammony and observed motions for celestial bodies, it is repeated in our memory. When it is repeated in our memory, an experience occurs from it due to a conjunction with memory, which is that if this thing (like purging due to scammony) were accidental and not due to its nature, it would not occur in most cases without variation. So if it is not found, the conscious soul seeks a reason for what occurred from its non-existence. So al-mujarrabat are propositions and judgments that follow our repeated observations.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines three more types of necessary propositions. First, al-fitriyyat (innate propositions): the intellect judges them with the aid of a necessary concomitant that is inseparable from the conception of the terms, hence called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them.' The example: 'four is even' – the concept of four necessarily implies divisibility into two equal parts. Second, al-mujarrabat (experiential): the intellect judges with repeated observation and a hidden syllogism that repeated uniform occurrence must have a cause, so that when the cause is known, the effect is known with certainty. Example: scammony purges bile. The footnote elaborates: repeated observation, combined with memory, leads to an experience that the relation is not accidental but natural; the mind seeks a cause if the effect is absent. Third, al-mutawatirat: judgments based on the multitude of reports from many reporters that preclude collusion. This covers the epistemic basis for knowledge from testimony.

Key Terms
الفطريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fitriyyat are based on pure intuition without any syllogism.
Correction: They involve a necessary concomitant, which is a form of reasoning though immediate.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them' mean?
A) The syllogism is externally added
B) The syllogism is embedded in the conception
C) They require repeated observation
D) They are based on testimony

Key Takeaway
Fitriyyat are necessary propositions that contain their own proof within the conception of their terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself that you consider self-evident. What ‘necessary concomitant’ is attached to your conception of yourself? Is that concomitant truly necessary, or could it be a false assumption? Will you examine that belief today?
?
When you judge someone else’s character quickly, what ‘syllogism with them’ are you using? Are you basing your judgment on a genuine necessary concomitant or on a hasty association? Commit to pausing before such judgments this week.

Experiential Propositions (Mujarrabat) – Knowledge from Repeated Observation and Hidden Syllogism
Sanusi defines mujarrabat and explains their epistemic basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mujarrabat are formed through repeated observation combined with a hidden syllogism that infers a causal connection.

Core Idea
Repeated observation plus a hidden syllogism yields certain knowledge of causality.

The second category relies on experience. When we repeatedly observe that one thing follows another, the intellect, aided by a hidden syllogism, concludes a causal relationship. Sanusi explains this using the example of scammony purging bile and elaborates on the psychological process.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mujarrabat
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part defining mujarrabat and the hidden syllogism.
2
Understand the hidden syllogism
The syllogism is: repeated uniform occurrence must have a cause, so when the cause is known the effect is known. This is a presupposition of induction.
Sanusi calls it 'al-qiyas al-khafi'.
3
Study the elaboration
Read the later paragraph that explains the psychological process: repeated observations lead to memory, and if the effect is absent, the mind seeks a cause, confirming the causal connection.
This paragraph clarifies why mujarrabat are certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفطريات فقضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة لا يعزب عنه عند تصور الطرف وهو المعنى بأمر لازم منضم إلى القضية، ولهذا تسمى قضايا قياساتها معها، كالحكم بأن الأربعة زوج، لانقسامها بمتساويين . وأما المجربات : فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بانضمام تكرر المشاهدة إليه ، والقياس الخفي المنتج لليقين إليها أن الوقوع المتكرر على نهج واحد لا بد له من سبب وإن لم يعرف ماهيته فكلما علم وجود السبب علم وجود المسبب قطعاً، وذلك كالحكم بأن السقمونيا مسهل للصفراء . وأما المتواترات فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة كثرة شهادة المخبرين . المجربات : أمور أوقع التصديق بها الحس بشركة القياس، وذلك أنه إذا تكرر في إحساسنا وجود شيء لشيء مثل الاسهال للسقموينا والحركات المرصودة للسماويات تكرر ذلك منا في الذكر. وإذا تكرر منا ذلك في الذكر، حدثت لنا منه تجربة بسبب اقتران بالذكر، وهو أنه لو كان هذا الأمر كالاسهال مثلاً عن السقمونيا اتفاقياً عرضياً لا عن مقتضى طبيعته لكان لا يكون في أكثر الأمر من غير اختلاف، حتى انه إذا لم يوجد ذلك استندت النفس الواقعة فطلبت سبباً لما عرض من أنه لم يوجد فالمجربات : قضايا وأحكام تتبع مشاهدات منا تتكرر.
As for al-fitriyyat (innate propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges by means of something that does not escape it when conceiving the term, meaning a necessary concomitant attached to the proposition. For this reason they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them, like the judgment that four is even because it is divisible into two equal parts. As for al-mujarrabat (experiential propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges with the addition of repeated observation to it, and the hidden syllogism that produces certainty to them: that repeated occurrence in one manner must have a cause even if its nature is not known; so whenever the cause is known, the effect is known with certainty. For example, the judgment that scammony is a purgative for bile. As for al-mutawatirat (recurrently reported propositions), they are propositions which the intellect judges by means of the multitude of reporters. Al-mujarrabat: Things whose affirmation is effected by sense with the participation of syllogism. That is, when in our sensation the existence of something for something is repeated, like purging for scammony and observed motions for celestial bodies, it is repeated in our memory. When it is repeated in our memory, an experience occurs from it due to a conjunction with memory, which is that if this thing (like purging due to scammony) were accidental and not due to its nature, it would not occur in most cases without variation. So if it is not found, the conscious soul seeks a reason for what occurred from its non-existence. So al-mujarrabat are propositions and judgments that follow our repeated observations.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines three more types of necessary propositions. First, al-fitriyyat (innate propositions): the intellect judges them with the aid of a necessary concomitant that is inseparable from the conception of the terms, hence called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them.' The example: 'four is even' – the concept of four necessarily implies divisibility into two equal parts. Second, al-mujarrabat (experiential): the intellect judges with repeated observation and a hidden syllogism that repeated uniform occurrence must have a cause, so that when the cause is known, the effect is known with certainty. Example: scammony purges bile. The footnote elaborates: repeated observation, combined with memory, leads to an experience that the relation is not accidental but natural; the mind seeks a cause if the effect is absent. Third, al-mutawatirat: judgments based on the multitude of reports from many reporters that preclude collusion. This covers the epistemic basis for knowledge from testimony.

Key Terms
المجربات
القياس الخفي
التجربة
المشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mujarrabat are based solely on sense experience without any reasoning.
Correction: They involve a hidden syllogism about causality, so they combine sense and reason.

Checkpoint
What is the hidden syllogism in mujarrabat?
A) Repeated events are random
B) Repeated uniform occurrence must have a cause
C) The senses are infallible
D) Memory is sufficient

Key Takeaway
Mujarrabat are certain propositions derived from repeated observation combined with a rational inference of causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the consistent cycles of day and night, do you merely accept them as a habit, or do you let them drive you to certainty about the One who ordains them? What would change in your worship if you treated every regularity in your life as a sign of His causation?
?
You trust that fire burns because you have seen it repeatedly. But do you trust Allah's promises with the same level of certainty? What repeated evidences in the Quran and creation have you observed, yet still doubt?
?
If a single exception to a pattern (like a miracle) makes you question the cause, what does that reveal about the depth of your conviction? Are you more persuaded by what you see with your eyes or by what Allah has revealed through His messengers?

Recurrently Reported Propositions (Mutawatirat) – Knowledge through Mass Testimony
Sanusi defines mutawatirat with their basis in multitude of reporters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mutawatirat provide certain knowledge through the multitude of reporters that precludes collusion.

Core Idea
Mass testimony from many reporters yields certain knowledge.

The third category of necessary propositions is based on testimony. When a report is transmitted by so many people that they could not have conspired to lie, the intellect accepts it as certain. This is the basis for knowledge of historical events and religious traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the one-sentence definition of mutawatirat and its translation. Notice the key condition: multitude of reporters (kathrat al-mukhberin).
2
Reflect on the epistemic mechanism
The intellect judges based on the multitude itself, not on the content. The impossibility of collusion is the basis.
3
Note the contrast with mujarrabat
Unlike mujarrabat which rely on repeated observation, mutawatirat rely on repeated reports from different people.

Al-mujarrabat: Things whose affirmation is effected by sense with the participation of syllogism. Sensory Propositions: External, Internal, and the Particularity of Sense
Author defines sensory propositions and divides them into external and internal, then asserts their particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sensory propositions, their division into external and internal types, and why sensory judgments are particular while universal judgments are rational.

Core Idea
Sensory judgments only capture particulars; universality is a rational attainment.

The author begins by defining a class of propositions known as sensory propositions (al-ḥissiyāt). He then divides them into two types—those derived from external senses and those from internal perception. Crucially, he explains that all sensory judgments are particular, meaning they only report on specific instances. The universal judgment that 'every fire is hot' is not sensory but rational, achieved through reasoning beyond the senses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the passage carefully and identify what sensory propositions are. Notice that the term 'sensory' applies to judgments based on direct experience.
The passage gives examples like 'the sun is bright' and 'fire is hot'.
2
Distinguish external vs internal
Look for the two categories: external sensory judgments (from outer senses) and internal sensory judgments, called introspective (wijdāniyyāt), such as feeling fear or anger.
Internal also includes self-awareness, which does not rely on bodily instruments.
3
Grasp the particularity of sensory judgments
The author states that all sensory judgments are particular. This means they only inform about a specific instance, not a general rule.
Example: 'this fire is hot' is particular, not 'all fire is hot'.
4
Recognize the rational universal
The judgment that 'every fire is hot' is not sensory but rational. It is formed with the help of sensing particular cases and understanding the causes.
This shows that reason is needed to move from particular observations to universal laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسمى حسيات كالحكم بأن الشمس نيرة والنار حارة، أو الباطنة وتسمى وجدانيات كالحكم بأن لنا خوفاً وغضباً، ومنها ما نجده بنفوسنا لا بالآلات البدنية كشعورنا بذواتنا وأحوالها، وجميع أحكام الحس جزئية لأنه لا يفيد إلا أن هذه النار حارة . وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة فحكم عقلي، حصل بمعونة الإحساس بجزئيات ذلك الحكم والوقوف على علله .
And it is called sensory propositions, such as the judgment that the sun is luminous and fire is hot, or the internal ones called introspective propositions, such as the judgment that we have fear and anger, and among them what we find in our souls, not by bodily instruments, like our awareness of ourselves and our states. And all sensory judgments are particular, because it does not convey except that this fire is hot. As for the judgment that every fire is hot, it is a rational judgment, arrived at with the help of sensing the particulars of that judgment and grasping its causes.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies sensory propositions (al-ḥissiyāt). The author first states that they are called sensory propositions, giving examples of external sensory judgments (e.g., the sun is bright, fire is hot). Then he distinguishes internal sensory propositions, called introspective propositions (wijdāniyyāt), such as the judgment that we have fear and anger. He notes that some of these are found in our souls without bodily instruments, like our awareness of ourselves and our states. He then emphasizes that all sensory judgments are particular: they only tell us about a specific instance (e.g., this fire is hot). The judgment that every fire is hot is a rational judgment, not a sensory one, attained with the aid of sensing particular instances and understanding the causes. Thus, sensory knowledge alone is restricted to the particular; the universal requires reason.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment 'every fire is hot' is a sensory judgment.
Correction: The author explicitly states that such a universal judgment is rational, not sensory. Sensory judgments only tell us about particular instances.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that all sensory judgments are particular?

B) Because sensory judgments are about internal states, not universals.
C) Because the judgment 'this fire is hot' does not prove all fire is hot.
D) Because sensory judgments are always about the present moment.

Key Takeaway
Sensory judgments are limited to particular instances; universal knowledge is a product of reason, not the senses alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel anger or fear, do you assume that the cause is universally true (e.g., 'this person is always wrong') or do you recognize it as a particular reaction? How can you discipline your mind to separate sensory particulars from rational universals?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself, like 'I always fail'. Is that a sensory particular (based on a few experiences) or a rational universal (based on understanding causes)? If it is sensory, what rational steps must you take to correct it?
?
The lesson says universal judgments require grasping causes ('ilal). Where in your life have you made a universal claim without truly understanding the causes? Commit to investigating the cause behind one such claim today.

Reduction of Proposition Types to Basic Categories
Author reduces types to basic ones and addresses verbal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author reduces all proposition types to a priori and observed propositions, and how the dispute about empirical and intuitive propositions is merely verbal.

Core Idea
All propositions ultimately rest on immediate certainty of a priori or observed knowledge.

In this passage, the author summarizes the classification of propositions by showing they can be reduced to two fundamental types: badīhiyyāt (a priori) and mushāhadāt (observed). He acknowledges that empirical and intuitive propositions involve reasoning, but scholars differ only in terminology. Let's read the Arabic and see how he makes this reduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about reduction
Read the Arabic passage from 'فهي وقد تنحصر' to 'والنزاع لفظي'. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two basic types
The author says propositions can be confined to badīhiyyāt (a priori) and mushāhadāt (observed). Note the two reasons given: either they encompass all, or the necessity of others is derived from them.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
The author mentions that empirical and intuitive propositions involve reasoning (naẓar), but investigators did not consider them theoretical (naẓariyyāt) but mediate (bil-wāsiṭa). The dispute is verbal (lafẓī). Ask yourself: What does 'mediate' mean here?
This connects to the idea that all knowledge ultimately goes back to immediate certainties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي وقد تنحصر في البديهيات والمشاهدات لشمولها الكل، أو لأن ضرورية ما سواهما، بل يقينية المجربات والحدسيات لا تخلو عن نظر، إلا أن المحققين منهم لم يجعلوه من النظريات بل بواسطة والنزاع لفظي، فإن قيل كيف ينازع في المتواتر وهو نوع من الحسي ..؟ قلنا : الكلام في مضمون الأخبار المسموعة كوجود مكة مثلاً. فقول الرواة : إنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «البيئة» على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» مسموع، والعلم
Thus they may be confined to a priori and observed propositions, because they encompass all, or because the necessity of what is other than them, rather certainties of empirical and intuitive propositions are not devoid of reasoning, except that the investigators among them did not consider them theoretical but rather mediate, and the dispute is verbal. For if it is said: How can one dispute about recurrent reports, while it is a type of sensory? We say: The discussion is about the content of the heard reports, such as the existence of Mecca. So the statement of the narrators: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath upon the denier”' is heard, and knowledge...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of proposition types by stating that they can be reduced to a priori (badīhiyyāt) and observed (mushāhadāt) categories, either because these encompass all or because the necessity of the others is derivative. The author notes that some scholars consider empirical and intuitive propositions to involve reasoning (naẓar), but the investigators among them do not consider them theoretical (naẓariyyāt) but rather mediate (bil-wāsiṭa), and the dispute is verbal. Then a question is raised: How can one dispute about recurrent reports (mutawātirāt) when they are a type of sensory proposition? The answer: The discussion concerns the content of the heard reports, such as the existence of Mecca. The narrators' statement that the Prophet said such-and-such is a heard (sensory) event, but the knowledge derived from it is not purely sensory; it involves the hidden syllogism of the impossibility of collusion. Thus the objection is based on a confusion between the sensory nature of the transmission and the rational inference involved.

Key Terms
بديهيات
مشاهدات
مجربات

نظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical and intuitive propositions are purely observational or intuitive without any reasoning.
Correction: The author states they are 'not devoid of reasoning' (لا تخلو عن نظر), but this reasoning is not independent; it is mediate.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the dispute about empirical and intuitive propositions is verbal?
A) Because they are all certain and need no classification.
B) Because the disagreement is only about whether they are considered theoretical or mediate, not about their epistemic status.
C) Because the scholars agree that they are purely a priori.
D) Because the author does not consider them at all.

Key Takeaway
All propositions reduce to a priori or observed knowledge; empirical and intuitive types involve reasoning but are mediated, making the dispute purely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's existence, do you rely more on a priori intuition (fitrah) or on observed evidence (creation)? Which of these is more certain for you, and why? Commit today to strengthening the foundation of your faith in that weaker area.
?
Consider a belief you hold about Islam that you think is purely empirical—like a historical event. Does your certainty come only from observation, or is it mediated through reports and reasoning? Are you willing to trace every conviction back to its root in a priori or observed knowledge, discarding any that rest on unexamined assumptions?
?
The passage says the dispute between scholars about empirical and intuitive knowledge is 'verbal'—they agree on reality. Are there theological disagreements you cling to that are merely verbal? Can you let go of pride and embrace the underlying truth, even if it means admitting you were arguing over wording?

The Objection Regarding Mutawātir Reports
Author answers objection about mutawātir being purely sensory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mutawātir reports are not purely sensory but involve a rational inference, and how this resolves the objection against their classification.

Core Idea
Mutawātir reports rely on sensory transmission but yield knowledge through a hidden syllogism.

The author addresses an objection: How can anyone dispute that mutawātir is a type of sensory knowledge? He explains that while the report itself is heard (sensory), the knowledge of its truth comes from a rational inference about the impossibility of collusion. This clarifies the verbal dispute. Let's examine the Arabic to see the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic from 'فإن قيل' to the end (though incomplete). Notice the objection: how can one dispute mutawātir when it is sensory? Then read the author's answer.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we have the core of the answer.
2
Separate sensory and rational elements
The author distinguishes between the sensory act of hearing the report (مسموع) and the knowledge of its content (مضمون الأخبار). The content, like the existence of Mecca, is known through a rational syllogism (impossibility of collusion).
3
Understand why the dispute is verbal
The objection conflates the sensory transmission with the rational inference. Once distinguished, it's clear that mutawātir involves both, so classifying it purely as sensory or rational is a matter of terminology.
This mirrors the earlier verbal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of proposition types by stating that they can be reduced to a priori (badīhiyyāt) and observed (mushāhadāt) categories, either because these encompass all or because the necessity of the others is derivative. The author notes that some scholars consider empirical and intuitive propositions to involve reasoning (naẓar), but the investigators among them do not consider them theoretical (naẓariyyāt) but rather mediate (bil-wāsiṭa), and the dispute is verbal. Then a question is raised: How can one dispute about recurrent reports (mutawātirāt) when they are a type of sensory proposition? The answer: The discussion concerns the content of the heard reports, such as the existence of Mecca. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious report or a hadith, do you passively accept it because of its sensory presence, or do you actively consider the rational basis that makes it trustworthy? Identify one report you have taken for granted and ask yourself: what hidden inference actually grounds my certainty?
?
The distinction between hearing words and understanding their truth involves both sensation and reason. Think of a time you believed something based on testimony. Can you separate the act of hearing from the logical step that assured you of its truth? How does that affect your humility toward transmitted knowledge?
?
This lesson shows that a verbal dispute can mask a real depth. Is there a belief you hold that you have classified as 'just sensory' or 'just obvious'? Could it be that your certainty actually rests on a rational inference you have never examined? Will you trace that inference today?
