Definition of Intuitive Judgments (Ḥadsiyyāt)
Definition of ḥadsiyyāt and comparison to mujarrabāt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what intuitive judgments (ḥadsiyyāt) are, how they differ from experiential judgments (mujarrabāt), and the role of intuition (ḥads) in attaining certainty.

Core Idea
Intuitive judgments are certain propositions reached by strong intuition from observed indications.

In this lesson, we explore a category of knowledge called ḥadsiyyāt (intuitive judgments). The author defines them as propositions that reason judges by a powerful intuition, eliminating doubt and yielding certainty through observed indications. Using the example of moonlight being reflected sunlight, he shows how intuition works. He then compares ḥadsiyyāt to mujarrabāt (experiential judgments), highlighting the difference in how the cause is known. This passage establishes the epistemological foundation for intuitive knowledge in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms used.
The passage is dense with logical terminology.
2
Identify the key characteristics of ḥadsiyyāt
Look for how the author defines ḥadsiyyāt: they are propositions judged by strong intuition (ḥads), eliminating doubt, resulting in certainty, and based on observed indications (qarā’in). The example of moonlight illustrates this.
3
Compare ḥadsiyyāt with mujarrabāt
Find the comparison between ḥadsiyyāt and mujarrabāt. Note the similarities (repeated observation, hidden syllogism) and the crucial difference: in mujarrabāt, the cause is known only in its causality, not its essence; in ḥadsiyyāt, it is known in both respects. Also note that the certainty in ḥadsiyyāt comes from intuition (ḥads) rather than reflective thought (fikr).
This distinction is important for understanding the classification of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحدسيات : فهي قضايا يحكم بها العقل بحدس قوي من النفس يزول معه الشك، ويحصل اليقين بمشاهدة القرائن كالحكم بأن نور القمر مستفاد من الشمس لما نرى من اختلاف تشکلات نوره بحسب اختلاف أوضاعه من الشمس، وذلك أنه يضيء دائماً جانبه الذي يلي الشمس وينتقل ضوؤه إلى مقابلة الشمس، فيحدس العقل بأنه لو لم يكن نوره من الشمس لما كان كذلك، كالمجربات في تكرر المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي إلا أن السبب في المجربات معلوم السببية غير معلوم الماهية. وفي الحدسيات معلوم بالوجهين إلا أن الوقوف عليه يكون بالحدس دون الفكر وإلا لكان من العلوم الكسبية، وستعرف معنى الحدس في بحث النفس .
As for intuitive judgments (al-ḥadsiyyāt): they are propositions that reason judges by a strong intuition (ḥads) of the soul, with which doubt disappears and certainty is obtained by observing the indications (qarā’in), such as the judgment that the light of the moon is derived from the sun, because we see the variation of its illumination according to its different positions relative to the sun, and that it always illuminates the side facing the sun and its light shifts opposite the sun; so reason intuits that if its light were not from the sun, it would not be so. They are like experiential judgments (al-mujarrabāt) in repetition of observation and concomitant hidden syllogism (qiyās khafī), except that in experiential judgments the cause is known in its causality but unknown in its essence, while in intuitive judgments it is known in both respects, except that ascertaining it is by intuition (ḥads) rather than reflection (fikr); otherwise it would be among acquired sciences (al-‘ulūm al-kasbiyya). And you will come to know the meaning of intuition in the discussion of the soul.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage defines a category of knowledge called ḥadsiyyāt (intuitive judgments). It states that these are propositions that reason judges through a powerful intuition (ḥads) from the soul that eliminates doubt and yields certainty. The key is that the judgment arises from observing indications (qarā’in). The example given is the deduction that moonlight is reflected sunlight: because we see the moon's illumination change with its position relative to the sun, and it always faces the sun, the mind intuits that if its light were independent, it wouldn't behave this way. The author then compares ḥadsiyyāt to mujarrabāt (experiential judgments): both involve repeated observation and a hidden syllogism. However, in mujarrabāt, the cause is known in terms of its causality (sababiyya) but not in its essence (māhiyya), whereas in ḥadsiyyāt the cause is known in both respects. The difference is that the certainty in ḥadsiyyāt is attained by intuition (ḥads) rather than reflective reasoning (fikr); if it were by reflection, it would be an acquired science (kasbī). The passage ends by promising further discussion of intuition in the section on the soul.

Key Terms
الحدسيات (al-ḥadsiyyāt)
الحدس (al-ḥads)
القياس الخفي (al-qiyās al-khafī)
المجربات (al-mujarrabāt)
القرائن (al-qarā’in)
السببية (al-sababiyya)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الكسبي (al-kasbī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think ḥadsiyyāt are based on pure reason without observation.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that ḥadsiyyāt are based on observing indications (qarā’in), as in the example of moonlight.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes ḥadsiyyāt from mujarrabāt according to this passage?
A) ḥadsiyyāt are based on sensory observation while mujarrabāt are not.
B) In ḥadsiyyāt the cause is known both in causality and essence, while in mujarrabāt only causality is known.
C) ḥadsiyyāt are certain while mujarrabāt are probabilistic.
D) The hidden syllogism is present in mujarrabāt but not in ḥadsiyyāt.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive judgments derive certainty from observing indications and differ from experiential judgments in that the cause is known both in causality and essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you felt absolutely certain about something without being able to explain why. According to this lesson, your certainty probably came from strong intuition supported by observed indications. Now ask yourself: were those observations real or imagined? What would it take for such certainty to be considered 'strong intuition' as defined here? Will you examine your deep-seated certainties today to see if they rely on genuine evidence?
?
The passage says that in intuitive judgments, the cause is known both in its causality and its essence. This means you know not only that something is caused, but you grasp the nature of that cause. In your own life, when you claim to know something, do you know only the surface (that it works) or do you understand the why? Which kind of knowledge do you settle for most often? What would change if you sought the essence behind the patterns you observe?
?
This lesson distinguishes intuition from reflective thinking, warning that if it were by reflection it would be an acquired science. Have you ever trusted your intuition over careful reasoning, and it led you astray? Or have you trusted reflection over deep gut-feeling, and regretted it? How can you tell which voice—intuition or reflection—is speaking the truth in a specific situation? Are you willing to test your intuitions against the standard of observed indications (qarā’in) described here?

The Nature of Mutawātir and the Verbal Dispute
Author addresses objection about mutawātir being sensory and clarifies the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute about mutawātir being sensory is verbal and how it relates to the classification of knowledge.

Core Idea
Mutawātir is sensory in hearing, but its content is inferential.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle point in the classification of certain knowledge. While some types involve reflection, they are not fully theoretical. An objection arises about mutawātir being sensory, and the answer reveals a key distinction between the sensory act of hearing and the content of reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts: the general classification statement and the objection-answer exchange.
The passage uses technical terms like badīhiyyāt and mushāhadāt.
2
Identify the classification of knowledge
The author says knowledge can be confined to primary axioms and observed things. Other types (experiential, intuitive) involve reflection but are not considered theoretical; the dispute is verbal.
This sets up the background for the objection.
3
Understand the objection
The objection asks: how can there be a dispute about mutawātir when it is a type of sensory knowledge? This seems contradictory.
The objection is that mutawātir is usually classified under sensory knowledge.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer distinguishes the sensory hearing of the report from the content. Knowledge of the report's authenticity (e.g., that it is a hadith) comes from tawātur, while knowledge of the content (e.g., the ruling) is inferred.
Thus mutawātir is not purely sensory in its content.
5
Reflect on the verbal dispute
The dispute is verbal because it hinges on what is meant by 'sensory'. The hearing is sensory, but the knowledge derived is not.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد تنحصر في البديهيات والمشاهدات لشمولها الكل، أو لأن ضرورية ما سواهما، بل يقينية المجربات والحدثيات لا تخلو عن نظر، إلا أن المحققين منهم لم يجعلوه من النظريات بل بواسطة والنزاع لفظي، فإن قيل كيف ينازع في المتواتر وهو نوع من الحسي ..؟ قلنا : الكلام في مضمون الأخبار المسموعة كوجود مكة مثلاً. فقول الرواة : إنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «البيئة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» مسموع، والعلم بأن هذا حديث النبي حاصل بالتواتر ضرورة أو غير ضرورة، وبأن البينة إنما تكون على المدعي حاصل بخبر الصادق استدلالاً).
He said: They can be confined to primary axioms (al-badīhiyyāt) and observed things (al-mushāhadāt) because they encompass all, or because what is necessary (ḍarūriyya) for other than them? Rather, the conclusiveness (yaqīniyya) of experiential judgments (al-mujarrabāt) and intuitive judgments (al-ḥadsiyyāt) is not free from reflection (naẓar), except that the investigators among them did not make them theoretical (naẓariyyāt), but by mediation, and the dispute is verbal. For if it is said: How can there be dispute about the mutawātir while it is a type of sensory (ḥissī)? We say: The discussion is about the content of the heard reports (maḍmūn al-akhbār al-masmū‘a), such as the existence of Mecca. So the statement of the narrators: ‘The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier”’ is heard, and the knowledge that this is a hadith of the Prophet is obtained by tawātur necessarily or not necessarily, and that the burden of proof is on the claimant is obtained by report of the truthful one by inference (istidlāl).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage begins by citing an unnamed authority (likely from a previous discussion) saying that the types of certain knowledge can be reduced to primary axioms and sensory observations, either because they cover all or because other types like mujarrabāt and ḥadsiyyāt involve some reflection, yet investigators don't consider them fully theoretical, so the dispute is verbal. Then an objection is raised: How can mutawātir be disputed when it is a type of sensory knowledge? The answer is that the dispute concerns the content (maḍmūn) of the reports, not the sensory act of hearing. The example: the report 'The burden of proof is on the claimant' is heard, but the knowledge that this is a genuine hadith is obtained through tawātur (mass transmission), and the ruling itself (burden of proof) is obtained by inference from a truthful report. Thus mutawātir is not purely sensory in its content.

Key Terms
المتواتر (al-mutawātir)
البديهيات (al-badīhiyyāt)
المشاهدات (al-mushāhadāt)
التواتر (al-tawātur)
الاستدلال (al-istidlāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think mutawātir is purely sensory knowledge because it is based on reports heard by many.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while the hearing itself is sensory, the content (e.g., the ruling or existence of Mecca) is known through inference or tawātur of the report, making the dispute about the content, not the sensory experience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there a dispute about mutawātir being sensory?
A) Because mutawātir is not based on senses.
B) Because the dispute is about the content of the reports, not the hearing.
C) Because mutawātir is theoretical knowledge.
D) Because the investigator said it is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
Mutawātir knowledge involves a sensory component (hearing), but the knowledge derived from it is inferential, resolving the verbal dispute about its classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a classification of knowledge as 'purely sensory' or 'purely rational' only to realize later that the dispute was merely verbal? How does this lesson humble your approach to scholarly disagreements? Will you now pause to ask whether a disagreement is about substance or terminology before taking sides?
?
The report 'The burden of proof is on the claimant' is heard sensorily, but its authority comes from mass transmission. In your own faith, are there teachings you accept because you 'heard' them, without tracing their chain of transmission? How can you move from sensory hearing to inferential certainty about divine commands?
?
When you encounter a classification dispute among scholars, do you tend to dismiss it as trivial or charge into one camp? This lesson shows that even a verbal dispute can clarify a deeper truth. What in your character needs to change so that you treat such disputes as opportunities for precision rather than occasions for division?

The First Apology: Broadening the Scope of Primary Axioms
First apology from al-Mawaqif expanding the scope of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first apology that reconciles al-Muhassal's limited definition of necessary knowledge by showing how primary axioms encompass innate judgments and how intuitive judgments include experiential and mutawatir knowledge.

Core Idea
The first apology expands primary axioms to include innate and experiential judgments.

In this passage, the author of al-Mawaqif offers an apology (defense) for al-Muhassal's classification that necessary knowledge is merely self-evident (wijdaniyyat). The first apology argues that primary axioms (badihiyyat) actually include innate judgments (fitriyyat) because the middle term is integral to the two extremes, so the mind only needs to conceive them. Additionally, intuitive judgments (hadiyyat) encompass experiential (mujarrabat) and mutawatir knowledge due to their reliance on repeated sensory input. This broadens the scope of what is considered necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first apology
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice that it begins by stating the problem (necessary knowledge limited to self-evident) and then presents the first of two apologies.
The text is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two components of the apology
The first apology has two parts: (1) how primary axioms include innate judgments, and (2) how intuitive judgments include experiential and mutawatir. Find each part in the text.
The key terms are 'الفطريات' and 'المجربات والمتواترات'.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each component
For innate judgments, the reason is that the middle term is necessary for conceiving the two extremes. For experiential and mutawatir, the reason is that the mind's judgment depends on sense but with repetition.
This shows that these types of knowledge are still necessary, just not purely self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر في المحصل أن الضروريات هي الوجدانيات، وأنها قليلة النفع في العلوم لكونها غير مشتركة والحسيات والبديهيات وتبعه صاحب المواقف إلا أنه ذكر في موضع آخر أن الضروريات هي الست المذكورة، والوهميات في المحسوسات كالحكم بأن كل جسم في جهة واعتذر لما في المحصل بوجهين : أحدهما: أن البديهيات تشمل الفطريات نظراً إلى أن الوسط لما كان لازماً لتصور الطرفين فكان العقل لم يفتقر إلا إلى تصورهما، والحدسيات تشمل المجربات والمتواترات، نظراً إلى استناد حكم العقل فيهما إلى الحس، لكن مع التكرر وكذا الحدسيات . وثانيهما: أن كون المجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات من قبيل الضروريات موضع بحث على ما فصله الإمام في الملخص، لاشتمال كل منها على ملاحظة قياس خفي، وكذا القضايا التي قياساتها معها، وكذا الحدسيات .
It was mentioned in al-Muḥaṣṣal that the necessary things (ḍarūriyyāt) are the self-evident (wijdāniyyāt), and that they are of little benefit in the sciences because they are not shared [by all], and [they are] the sensory (ḥissiyyāt) and primary axioms (badīhiyyāt). The author of al-Mawāqif followed him, except that he mentioned elsewhere that the necessary things are the six mentioned, and [also] the estimative (wahmiyyāt) in sensible things like the judgment that every body is in a direction. And he apologized for what is in al-Muḥaṣṣal with two aspects: First: that primary axioms include innate (fiṭriyyāt) in view of the fact that the middle term, since it is necessary for conceiving the two extremes, the mind does not need anything but conceiving them. And intuitive judgments (ḥadsiyyāt) include experiential (mujarrabāt) and tawātur (mutawātirāt), in view of the fact that the judgment of reason depends on sense in both, but with repetition. And likewise intuitive judgments. Second: that the status of experiential, mutawātir, and intuitive as being of the type of necessary things is a subject of investigation, as detailed by the Imām in al-Mulakhkhaṣ, because each includes a hidden syllogism (qiyās khafī), and likewise propositions whose syllogisms are with them, and likewise intuitive judgments.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a reference to al-Razi's al-Muhassal and al-Iji's al-Mawaqif. It states that in al-Muhassal, necessary knowledge was identified as the self-evident (wijdaniyyat) and that they are of little benefit because they are not universal. The author of al-Mawaqif followed this but also mentioned elsewhere that the necessary are the six categories and includes wahmiyyat. Then he offers two apologies (wajhayn) for the position in al-Muhassal. The first apology: primary axioms (badihiyyat) already include innate (fitriyyat) because the middle term is necessary for conceiving the two terms, so the mind only needs to conceive them. And hadsiyyat include mujarrabat and mutawatirat because the judgment of reason depends on sense but with repetition. The second apology: the classification of mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and hadsiyyat as necessary is disputed, as detailed by Imam al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas, because each involves a hidden syllogism. So they are not purely necessary.

Key Terms
البديهيات (badīhiyyāt)
الفطريات (fiṭriyyāt)
الحدسيات (ḥadsiyyāt)
المجربات (mujarrabāt)
المتواترات (mutawātirāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that innate judgments (fitriyyat) are a separate category from primary axioms (badihiyyat) and not necessary.
Correction: The first apology shows that innate judgments are actually included in primary axioms because the middle term is inherent in the conception of the two extremes, so they are indeed necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first apology, why do primary axioms include innate judgments?
A) Because innate judgments are based on sense.
B) Because the middle term is necessary for conceiving the two extremes, so the mind only needs to conceive them.
C) Because innate judgments are actually theoretical.
D) Because the mind needs reflection.

Key Takeaway
The first apology shows that necessary knowledge is broader than just self-evident truths, encompassing innate, experiential, and mutawatir knowledge through a broader understanding of primary axioms and intuitive judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim certainty in your faith, but which category from this lesson (sensory, innate, experiential, mutawātir) grounds your deepest beliefs? Can you honestly say you have internalized them to the point of necessity? Commit to repeating one core belief daily until it becomes as certain as an innate judgment.
?
Think of a time you rejected a truth because it didn't fit your narrow definition of certainty—perhaps a lesson from repeated life experience or a reliable report. How did that resistance harm your understanding? Will you now open yourself to the broader scope of necessary knowledge this passage teaches?
?
The first apology shows that necessity can arise through repetition. Is there a teaching of Allah or His Messenger that you have neglected because you haven't repeated it until it became a part of you? Write down one such teaching and make a plan to meditate on it until your heart is compelled.

The Second Apology: Hidden Syllogisms Question Necessity
Second apology questioning the necessity of experiential, tawatur, and intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second apology that questions whether experiential, mutawatir, and intuitive judgments are truly necessary, because each involves a hidden syllogism according to Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
Hidden syllogisms challenge the necessity of experiential and intuitive knowledge.

The second apology takes a more critical stance: the classification of experiential (mujarrabat), tawatur (mutawatirat), and intuitive (hadiyyat) judgments as necessary is not settled. As detailed by Imam al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas, each of these contains a hidden syllogism (qiyas khafi), meaning they are not purely self-evident but depend on an implicit reasoning process. This forces theologians to re-evaluate what counts as necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the second apology passage
Read the Arabic text for the second apology. Notice that it begins with 'وثانيهما' (and the second) and presents a different perspective.
This part is shorter but dense.
2
Identify the claim about hidden syllogism
The apology states that experiential, mutawatir, and intuitive judgments are each a subject of investigation because they involve a hidden syllogism. Find the phrase 'القياس الخفي' in the text.
This is the key concept.
3
Understand the implication for necessity
If a judgment involves a hidden syllogism, it is not purely necessary but depends on reasoning. This means these types may not belong to the category of necessary knowledge.
This contrasts with the first apology which tried to include them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر في المحصل أن الضروريات هي الوجدانيات، وأنها قليلة النفع في العلوم لكونها غير مشتركة والحسيات والبديهيات وتبعه صاحب المواقف إلا أنه ذكر في موضع آخر أن الضروريات هي الست المذكورة، والوهميات في المحسوسات كالحكم بأن كل جسم في جهة واعتذر لما في المحصل بوجهين : أحدهما: أن البديهيات تشمل الفطريات نظراً إلى أن الوسط لما كان لازماً لتصور الطرفين فكان العقل لم يفتقر إلا إلى تصورهما، والحدسيات تشمل المجربات والمتواترات، نظراً إلى استناد حكم العقل فيهما إلى الحس، لكن مع التكرر وكذا الحدسيات . وثانيهما: أن كون المجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات من قبيل الضروريات موضع بحث على ما فصله الإمام في الملخص، لاشتمال كل منها على ملاحظة قياس خفي، وكذا القضايا التي قياساتها معها، وكذا الحدسيات .
It was mentioned in al-Muḥaṣṣal that the necessary things (ḍarūriyyāt) are the self-evident (wijdāniyyāt), and that they are of little benefit in the sciences because they are not shared [by all], and [they are] the sensory (ḥissiyyāt) and primary axioms (badīhiyyāt). The author of al-Mawāqif followed him, except that he mentioned elsewhere that the necessary things are the six mentioned, and [also] the estimative (wahmiyyāt) in sensible things like the judgment that every body is in a direction. And he apologized for what is in al-Muḥaṣṣal with two aspects: First: that primary axioms include innate (fiṭriyyāt) in view of the fact that the middle term, since it is necessary for conceiving the two extremes, the mind does not need anything but conceiving them. And intuitive judgments (ḥadsiyyāt) include experiential (mujarrabāt) and tawātur (mutawātirāt), in view of the fact that the judgment of reason depends on sense in both, but with repetition. And likewise intuitive judgments. Second: that the status of experiential, mutawātir, and intuitive as being of the type of necessary things is a subject of investigation, as detailed by the Imām in al-Mulakhkhaṣ, because each includes a hidden syllogism (qiyās khafī), and likewise propositions whose syllogisms are with them, and likewise intuitive judgments.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a reference to al-Razi's al-Muhassal and al-Iji's al-Mawaqif. It states that in al-Muhassal, necessary knowledge was identified as the self-evident (wijdaniyyat) and that they are of little benefit because they are not universal. The author of al-Mawaqif followed this but also mentioned elsewhere that the necessary are the six categories and includes wahmiyyat. Then he offers two apologies (wajhayn) for the position in al-Muhassal. The first apology: primary axioms (badihiyyat) already include innate (fitriyyat) because the middle term is necessary for conceiving the two terms, so the mind only needs to conceive them. And hadsiyyat include mujarrabat and mutawatirat because the judgment of reason depends on sense but with repetition. The second apology: the classification of mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and hadsiyyat as necessary is disputed, as detailed by Imam al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas, because each involves a hidden syllogism. So they are not purely necessary.

Key Terms
المجربات (mujarrabāt)
المتواترات (mutawātirāt)
الحدسيات (ḥadsiyyāt)
القياس الخفي (al-qiyās al-khafī)
الملخص (al-Mulakhkhaṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that experiential knowledge (like 'fire burns') is purely necessary because it seems self-evident from experience.
Correction: The second apology points out that such knowledge involves a hidden syllogism (implicit reasoning from repeated observations), so it is not purely necessary but theoretical.

Checkpoint
According to the second apology, why is the classification of experiential, mutawatir, and intuitive judgments as necessary open to investigation?
A) Because they are not based on sense.
B) Because they involve a hidden syllogism.
C) Because they are not shared by all.
D) Because they require reflection.

Key Takeaway
The second apology reveals that experiential, tawatur, and intuitive knowledge are not necessarily necessary, as each contains a hidden syllogism that requires investigation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an experience you've had multiple times that you now consider certain—like the taste of a particular food or the reaction of a friend. Is it possible that your certainty is based on a hidden syllogism you never examined? What would change if you questioned it?
?
How many of your beliefs about Islam are based on repetition or widespread acceptance (tawatur) without critical thought? Are you willing to investigate the hidden reasoning behind them, even if it challenges your comfort?

Dispute on the Certainty of Experiential and Intuitive Knowledge
Author reports dispute and introduces investigators' intermediate view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly dispute over whether experiential (mujarrabāt) and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt) judgments are conclusive or merely probable, and the investigators' view that they are neither necessary nor theoretical.

Core Idea
Some certain knowledge is neither necessary nor theoretical but intermediate.

Not all forms of certain knowledge fit neatly into the categories of necessary or theoretical. This passage highlights a debate about two types—experiential and intuitive—and introduces the position of investigators who argue for an intermediate status. Read on to see how this dispute shapes the classification of knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: mujarrabāt, ḥadsiyyāt, yaqīniyyāt, ẓanniyyāt, ḍarūriyyāt, naẓariyyāt.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the dispute
The passage states that some scholars disputed whether mujarrabāt and ḥadsiyyāt are even conclusive (yaqīniyyāt), let alone necessary. Many scholars considered ḥadsiyyāt to be merely probable (ẓanniyyāt).
This shows a range of opinions on the epistemic status of these types.
3
Understand the investigators' position
The investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) hold that these four types (presumably mujarrabāt, ḥadsiyyāt, and others) are not necessary (ḍarūriyyāt) but also not theoretical (naẓariyyāt). They occupy an intermediate category.
This challenges the binary classification of knowledge into necessary and theoretical.

This passage presents a dispute: some scholars deny that mujarrabat and hadsiyyat are even conclusive (yaqiniyyat), let alone necessary (daruriyyat). In fact, many scholars classify hadsiyyat as merely probable (zanniyyat). Then the author mentions the position of the investigators (muhaqqiqun) who say that these four categories (presumably mujarrabat, mutawatirat, hadsiyyat, and maybe something else) are neither necessary nor theoretical, but something else. The passage is cut off but gives the key claim that these types occupy an intermediate status.

Correction: The passage shows that investigators hold that experiential and intuitive judgments are certain yet neither necessary nor theoretical, implying an intermediate category.

?

?

When it comes to your own convictions, are you an investigator who pushes beyond dichotomies, or do you settle for comfortable categories? The Six Types of Necessary Knowledge
Introduces two scholars' lists of necessary knowledge and a first reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of necessary knowledge and how scholars initially classified them.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge ranges from self-evident truths to widely reported facts.

Scholars debate what counts as 'necessary' knowledge. This lesson introduces the classification from al-Muhassal and al-Mawaqif, and the first attempt to reconcile their lists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on classification
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two scholars' lists and the first reconciliation.
Al-Muhassal limits necessary to self-evident; al-Mawaqif expands to six types.
2
Identify the six types
While the passage doesn't list all six explicitly, it mentions: al-wijdaniyyat, al-hissiyyat, al-badihiyyat, al-fitriyyat, al-hadsiyyat, al-mujarrabat, al-mutawatirat. Count them.
Notice the overlap and the reconciliation that includes fitriyyat under badihiyyat.
3
Understand the first reconciliation
The first excuse expands badihiyyat to include fitriyyat, and hadsiyyat to include mujarrabat and mutawatirat.
This is a way to mesh the two classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر في المحصل أن الضروريات هي الوجدانيات، وأنها قليلة النفع في العلوم لكونها غير مشتركة والحسيات والبديهيات وتبعه صاحب المواقف إلا أنه ذكر في موضع آخر أن الضروريات هي الست المذكورة، والوهميات في المحسوسات كالحكم بأن كل جسم في جهة واعتذر لما في المحصل بوجهين : أحدهما: أن البديهيات تشمل الفطريات نظراً إلى أن الوسط لما كان لازماً لتصور الطرفين فكان العقل لم يفتقر إلا إلى تصورهما، والحدسيات تشمل المجربات والمتواترات، نظراً إلى استناد حكم العقل فيهما إلى الحس، لكن مع التكرر وكذا الحدسيات . وثانيهما: أن كون المجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات من قبيل الضروريات موضع بحث على ما فصله الإمام في الملخص ، لاشتمال كل منها على ملاحظة قياس خفي، وكذا القضايا التي قياساتها معها، ونازع بعضهم في كون المجربات والحدسيات من قبيل اليقينيات، فضلاً عن كونها ضرورية بل جعل كثير من العلماء الحدسيات من قبيل الظنيات ثم المحققون من القائلين هذه الأربعة ليست من الضروريات على أنها ليست من النظريات أيضاً بل واسطة لعدم افتقارها إلى الاكتساب الفكري، وبهذا يشعر كلام الإمام حجة الإسلام حيث قال : العلم الحاصل بالتواتر ضروري بمعنى أنه لا يحتاج إلى الشعور بتوسط واسطة مفضية إليه مع أن الواسطة حاضرة في الذهن، وليس ضرورياً بمعنى الحاصل من غير واسطة كما في قولنا : الموجود ليس بمعدوم فإنه لا بد فيه من حصول مقدمتين . إحداهما : أن هؤلاء مع كثرتهم، واختلاف أحوالهم لا يجمعهم على الكذب جامع . الثانية : أنهم قد اتفقوا على الإخبار عن الواقعة لكنه لا يفتقر إلى ترتيب المقدمتين ولا إلى الشعور بتوسطهما ، ولا بإفضائهما إليه . وبهذا يظهر أن النزاع لفظي مبني على تفسير الضروري، أنه الذي لا يفتقر إلى واسطة أصلاً، أو الذي نجد أنفسنا مضطرين إليه .
He mentioned in al-Muhassal that the necessary things are the self-evident things (al-wijdaniyyat), and that they are of little benefit in the sciences because they are not common, and the sensed things and the self-evident things. And the author of al-Mawaqif followed him, except that he mentioned elsewhere that the necessary things are the six mentioned, and the imaginary things in sensed things, like the judgment that every body is in a direction. And he apologized for what was in al-Muhassal in two ways: First: that the self-evident things (al-badihiyyat) include the innate things (al-fitriyyat) considering that when the middle term is necessary for the conception of the two extremes, the intellect did not need except to conceive them, and the intuitive things (al-hadsiyyat) include the empirical things (al-mujarrabat) and the mutawatir things, considering that the intellect's judgment in them is based on sense, but with repetition, and likewise the intuitive things. Second: that the fact that the empirical, mutawatir, and intuitive things are of the type of necessary things is a subject of investigation, as the Imam detailed in al-Mulakhkhas, because each of them includes observing a hidden syllogism, and likewise propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And some disputed whether the empirical and intuitive things are of the type of certainties, let alone being necessary; rather, many scholars made the intuitive things of the type of probabilistic things. Then the investigators among those who say that these four are not from the necessary things, yet they are not from the theoretical things either, but an intermediate because they do not require intellectual acquisition. And this is suggested by the statement of Imam Hujjat al-Islam, who said: The knowledge obtained through tawatur is necessary in the sense that it does not require awareness of a mediating means leading to it, while the means is present in the mind, and it is not necessary in the sense of being obtained without a means, as in our saying: 'The existent is not non-existent', for it necessarily requires obtaining two premises: one: that these people, despite their multitude and differing conditions, no common factor gathers them upon lying. The second: that they have agreed upon reporting about the event, but it does not require arranging the two premises nor awareness of their intermediation nor of their leading to it. And with this it becomes clear that the dispute is verbal, based on the interpretation of 'the necessary' (al-daruri): whether it is that which does not require any intermediary at all, or that which we find ourselves compelled to.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of necessary knowledge (al-daruriyyat). It opens by reporting from al-Muhassal that necessary things are essentially the self-evident things (al-wijdaniyyat), and notes their limited usefulness because they are not shared across all sciences. The author of al-Mawaqif initially agreed but later gave a list of six types of necessary knowledge, including imaginary judgments about sensed things. Two excuses are offered for the position in al-Muhassal. First, that self-evident things (al-badihiyyat) actually include innate things (al-fitriyyat) because the middle term is inherent in the conception of the extremes, and intuitive things (al-hadsiyyat) include empirical and mutawatir things because they rely on sense with repetition. Second, that whether empirical, mutawatir, and intuitive things are necessary is debatable, as each involves a hidden syllogism. Some scholars even deny that empirical and intuitive things are certain, let alone necessary, with many regarding intuitive things as merely probable. The 'investigators' hold that these four types (al-mujarrabat, al-mutawatirat, al-hadsiyyat, and al-fitriyyat?) are not necessary nor theoretical, but intermediate because they do not require intellectual acquisition (iktisab fikri). This is supported by al-Ghazali's statement that knowledge from tawatur is necessary in the sense that it does not require awareness of a mediating means, even though such a means is present in the mind. He gives the example of the two premises behind tawatur: the impossibility of collusion and the agreement on the report, which are not consciously arranged. The passage concludes that the entire dispute is verbal, turning on whether 'necessary' means 'without any intermediary' or 'that which we feel compelled by'.

Key Terms
الضروريات
الوجدانيات
البديهيات
الفطريات
الحدسيات
المجربات
المتواترات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first reconciliation, what do al-badihiyyat include?
A) Al-wijdaniyyat only
B) Al-fitriyyat
C) Al-mujarrabat
D) Al-mutawatirat

Key Takeaway
Scholars initially differed on the scope of necessary knowledge, but the first reconciliation expands self-evident and intuitive categories to cover the six types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how scholars worked to reconcile differing views on necessary knowledge. Is there a belief you hold with such certainty that you have never questioned its grounds? What would it cost you to examine its foundation tonight?
?
The first excuse expands self-evident knowledge to include innate knowledge. Think of something you have always 'just known' about your Creator or your purpose. Have you been living as if that knowledge is real, or have you buried it under layers of distraction and doubt? What one step can you take today to act on that innate knowledge?
?
Consider the empirical knowledge you rely on daily—from science to news. The passage reminds you that such knowledge is intuitive but requires repetition. When was the last time you critically evaluated a piece of empirical information before trusting it with your life decisions? Will you let the next piece of repeated information shape your eternity without verification?

The Verbal Dispute on Necessary Knowledge
Continues the debate on necessary knowledge, presents intermediate category, and concludes with a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classification of certain necessary knowledge types is a verbal dispute and how al-Ghazali's analysis resolves it.

Core Idea
The dispute over necessary knowledge hinges on the definition of 'necessary'.

Scholars debate whether empirical, mutawatir, and intuitive knowledge are truly necessary or even certain. Some propose an intermediate category. Al-Ghazali's famous explanation shows that the dispute is verbal, based on two meanings of 'necessary'.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate section
Read the Arabic passage from 'Some disputed' to the end, then the translation. Note the different positions.
The passage presents a spectrum of views: some deny certainty, some say intermediate, and Ghazali clarifies.
2
Identify the intermediate category
The investigators say these four types are neither necessary nor theoretical, but an intermediate class because they do not require intellectual acquisition.
This is a key nuance.
3
Study al-Ghazali's analysis
Al-Ghazali distinguishes two senses of 'necessary': without any intermediary vs. compelled without conscious arrangement of premises.
His example of tawatur: the premises are present but not explicitly used.
4
Grasp the verbal dispute conclusion
The passage concludes that the entire dispute is verbal, turning on which definition of 'necessary' one adopts.

?

Objection and Answer on Mutawatirat as Sensed Knowledge
Responds to the objection that mutawatir reports are sensed and thus necessarily known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mutawatir reports are not purely sensory knowledge and why the content of such reports is considered acquired (iktisabi) knowledge.

Core Idea
Mutawatir knowledge is not merely sensory; its content requires intellectual reasoning.

A common objection in kalam is that mutawatir reports are known through hearing, so they should be necessary knowledge like any sensory experience. But a careful distinction between the sound itself and the content of the report shows that the knowledge of the content is intellectual and acquired. This passage presents that objection and the beginning of its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that mutawatirat are sensed and thus necessary, like knowing fire is hot.
The objection considers hearing the reports as similar to direct sensory experience.
2
Read the answer, noting the distinction
The answer distinguishes between the sensory sound (heard) and the intellectual content (ma‘qul) of the report.
The discussion is about the content (madhmun), not the sound itself.

The answer uses examples: knowing Mecca exists from tawatur, and knowing a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ).

Note the incomplete argument
The passage cuts off mid-sentence after stating one type of knowledge (sensory) and beginning to discuss knowledge of the report's truth.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding mutawatir reports. The objection claims that mutawatirat are sensed through hearing, so knowledge of them should be necessary (daruri) without dispute, just like knowing that fire is hot. The answer makes a crucial distinction: the discussion concerns knowledge of the content (madhmun) of the mutawatir report, not the sound itself. For example, knowing that Mecca exists or that the Prophet said a specific hadith is intellectual (ma‘qul), not purely sensory. The means to this knowledge is repeated hearing (takarru‘ al-samā‘). The answer then gives an example: if reports are mutawatir that the Prophet said 'al-bayyina ‘alā man idda‘ā…', the knowledge that this is the voice of the reporters is sensory, but the knowledge that the report is truthful involves a syllogism (though not consciously arranged). The passage is cut off before completing the explanation of the second kind of knowledge.

Answering the Objection: Mutawatirat as Sensory vs. Inferential
Al-Taftazani records the objection that mutawatirat are sensory and answers by distinguishing the subject of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani responds to the objection that mutawatir knowledge is purely sensory by distinguishing between the sensory perception of the sound and the inferential knowledge of the content.

Core Idea
Mutawatir reports involve inference, not direct sensation.

This passage presents a critical objection in kalam: if mutawatir reports are perceived through hearing, shouldn't the resulting knowledge be necessary like feeling heat? Al-Taftazani's answer shows that while the sound is sensory, the content—such as the existence of Mecca or the authority of a hadith—requires an inferential step. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating how transmitted knowledge fits into the framework of necessary vs. acquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector equates mutawatirat with sensory knowledge. Notice the example of fire being hot.
2
Identify the distinction in the answer
Al-Taftazani separates knowledge of the sound (sensory) from knowledge of the content (acquired through premises).
3
Analyze the example of the hadith
The hadith about evidence and oath is used to show how the content is derived from two premises.
4
Understand the final clarification
The passage notes that calling a hadith mutawatir refers to the transmission, not that the content is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: المتواترات من قبيل المحسوسات بحس السمع فيجب أن يكون ضرورياً بلا نزاع، كالعلم بأن النار حارة. قلنا: الكلام في العلم بمضمون الخبر المسموع تواتر كوجود مكة مثلا، وهو معقول. آلته بتكرر السماع حتى إذا كان المسموع المتواتر خبراً عن نسبته خبر إلى صدق كان العلم بمضمون ذلك الخبر اكتسابياً وفاقاً، مثلاً إذا تواترت الأخبار بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال «البينة» على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر. فالعلم بأن هذا صوت المخبرين مأخوذ من الحس والعلم بأن الخبر المنقول كلام النبي هو المستفاد من القضية التي من قبيل المتواترات المتنازع في أنه ضروري أو غير ضروري والعلم بأن البينة تجب على المدعي كسبي مستفاد من ترتيب المقدمتين أعني أن هذا خبر النبي عليه السلام وكل ما هو خبر عن النبي عليه السلام فمضمونه حق، لما ثبت من صدقه بدلالة المعجزات وما يقال من أن هذا الحديث متواتر فمعناه أن الخبر يكونه كلام النبي متواتر، سواء كان هو في نفسه خبراً أو إنشاء.
For if it is said: Mutawatirat are of the type of sensory objects through the sense of hearing, so they must be necessary without dispute, like the knowledge that fire is hot. We say: The discussion concerns knowledge of the content of a report heard through tawatur, like the existence of Mecca for example, and it is intelligible. Its instrument is repetition of hearing such that if the mutawatir heard report is a report about the attribution of a report to truthfulness, then knowledge of the content of that report is acquired by agreement. For example, if reports are mutawatir that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the denier.' Then knowledge that this is the voice of the reporters is taken from sense, and knowledge that the transmitted report is the speech of the Prophet is derived from the proposition which is of the type of mutawatirat disputed as to whether it is necessary or not necessary, and knowledge that the evidence is obligatory upon the claimant is acquired, derived from the arrangement of the two premises, namely that this is a report of the Prophet ﷺ, and everything that is a report of the Prophet ﷺ, its content is true, due to the establishment of his truthfulness by the evidence of miracles. And what is said that this hadith is mutawatir means that the report is the speech of the Prophet mutawatir, whether it is in itself a report or a directive.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that mutawatir reports should be considered necessary knowledge without dispute because they are perceived through the sense of hearing, like the direct sensory knowledge that fire is hot. The answer distinguishes between two types of knowledge: (1) knowledge of the sound of the reporters (which is sensory and necessary), and (2) knowledge of the content of the report (which involves inference). The author explains that knowledge of the content, e.g., the existence of Mecca, is intelligible and its instrument is repeated hearing. However, when the report itself is about the truthfulness of a statement, as in the hadith about evidence and oath, the knowledge of the content is acquired by arranging premises: that this is a report of the Prophet and that every report of the Prophet is true due to miracles. The author notes that the claim that the hadith is mutawatir refers to the transmission of the report itself, not necessarily its content. Thus, the objection fails because it conflates the sensory perception of the sound with the inference of the meaning.

محسوسات
اكتسابي
ضروري
مقدمتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mutawatir knowledge is purely sensory like feeling heat.
Correction: The passage shows it involves an inferential step from transmitted reports.

Checkpoint

B) Knowledge of the sound is sensory, knowledge of the content is acquired through premises.

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir knowledge requires an inferential step from sensory perception of the sound to reasoning about the content.

The Skeptical Objection and the Reply
Al-Taftazani presents the skeptical objection and the reply, then states the agreement of Ahl al-Haqq and the three groups of deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptical objection to sensory knowledge and the reply that it does not negate certainty in cases free from error.

Core Idea
Sensory error does not disprove sensory certainty.

Al-Taftazani begins by addressing a common skeptical challenge: that the senses often err, so they cannot be trusted. He replies that this does not affect cases where error is absent. He then notes that the people of truth agree that sensory and self-evident truths are first principles for proof. However, some groups denied this in various ways.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Examine the initial quote: some deniers impugn sensory objects because sense often errs. The reply is that this does not negate certain correspondence in that which has no error.
2
Understand the agreement of Ahl al-Haqq
Note that the people of truth agree that sensory and self-evident truths are first principles for proof. This is the established position.
3
Identify the three groups of deniers
Find the three groups: those who impugned sensory objects only, those who impugned self-evidents only, and those who impugned both.
4
Reflect on the refutation of philosophers
Consider al-Razi's refutation: even the major philosophers' certain sciences depend on sensory premises, so they cannot truly deny sensory certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المنكرون فمنهم من قدح في الحسيـات بأن الحس قد يغلط كثيراً، والجواب أنه لا ينافي الجزم المطابق فيما لا غلط فيه). أقول قد ثبت اتفاق أهل الحق على أن الحسيات والبديهيات مبادىء أول لما يقوم حجة على الغير، وأنكر ذلك جماعة فمنهم من قدح في الحسيات وحصر المبادىء الأول في البديهيات ومنهم من عكس، ومنهم من قدح فيهما جميعاً، ولكل من الفرق شبه، وقد أطنب الإمام فيها بتكثير الأمثلة، ونسب القول بعدم كون الحسيات من اليقينيات إلى أكابر الفلاسفة، ورد بأن علومهم اليقينية مبنية عليها، والمبادىء الضرورية مستندة إليها، على ما صرحوا بأن مبادىء المجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات هي الإحساس بالجزئيات، وأن الأوليات يكتسبها الصبيان باستعداد يحصل لعقولهم من الإحساس بالجزئيات، فكيف ينسب إليهم القول بأنها ليست يقينية؟ واعتذر بأن المراد أن جزم العقل بالأحكام المأخوذة من الحس قد تتوقف على شرائط، ربما لا يعلم ماهي. ومتى حصلت؟ وكيف حصلت؟ فلذلك جعلوا لبيان مواضع الغلط في
He said: 'As for the deniers, among them are those who impugned the sensory objects by saying that sense often errs, and the answer is that this does not negate certain correspondence in that which has no error.' I say: It has been established by agreement of the people of truth that the sensory and self-evident are first principles for establishing proof against others, but a group denied that. Among them are those who impugned sensory objects and restricted the first principles to self-evidents; among them are those who did the opposite; and among them are those who impugned both. Each of the groups has specious arguments. The Imam elaborated on them with many examples, and attributed the statement that sensory objects are not certainties to the major philosophers, and refuted it by saying that their certain sciences are built upon them, and the necessary principles are dependent on them, as they stated that the principles of experiments, mutawatirat, and intuitions are sensory perception of particulars, and that the self-evidents are acquired by children through a disposition that occurs in their minds from sensory perception of particulars. So how can the statement that they are not certain be attributed to them? And he excused by saying that the intention is that the mind's certainty regarding judgments taken from sense may depend on conditions which perhaps are not known as to what they are, when they occur, and how they occur. Therefore they made it for explaining the places of error in
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage addresses the denial of sensory knowledge as a source of certainty. It begins with a quote: some deniers attack sensory knowledge by pointing out that the senses often err, but the reply is that this does not negate certain correspondence in cases free from error. The author then states that the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) agree that sensory (hissiyyat) and self-evident (badihiyyat) truths are first principles for proof. Some groups denied this: some rejected sensory knowledge and only accepted self-evident; some did the opposite; some rejected both. The author mentions that Imam al-Razi elaborated on this and attributed the denial of sensory certainty to major philosophers, but refuted it by showing that even their own certain sciences are built on sensory premises. For example, the principles of experiments, mutawatirat, and intuitions all rely on sensory perception of particulars, and even self-evident truths are acquired by children through a disposition arising from sensory perception. Therefore, it is inconsistent to say these are not certain. The author notes an excuse: the intention is that the mind's certainty from sense may depend on unknown conditions, so they were merely explaining where error can occur, not denying certainty altogether.

Key Terms
حسيات
بديهيات
يقينيات
مبادىء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows that occasional errors do not affect cases where error is absent, and that even skeptics rely on sensory premises.

Checkpoint
What is the reply to the objection that the senses often err?
A) Senses are always reliable and never err.
B) Error does not negate certainty in cases free from error.
C) Sensory knowledge is not needed for proof.
D) Self-evident truths are more important.

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge is foundational and not disproven by occasional errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The philosophers whose sciences depend on sensory data still denied sensory certainty. Is there an area in your life where your actions contradict your professed beliefs? What would it cost you to bring them into alignment?

The Excuse for Philosophers' Denial
Al-Taftazani explains an excuse for the philosophers' position, clarifying that they only denied unconditional sensory certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's explanation of why the philosophers' denial of sensory certainty was only about conditional cases, not absolute.

Core Idea
Philosophers meant that sensory certainty depends on unknown conditions.

In the previous lesson, we saw al-Taftazani defend sensory knowledge. Here he addresses the philosophers' stance: they attributed the denial to them, but then excuses it by saying they meant that certainty from sense may depend on conditions that are not fully known. This lesson explores that excuse.

Guided Path
1
Read the excuse carefully
The excuse is that the philosophers intended to say that the mind's certainty from sensory judgments depends on conditions that may not be known.
2
Understand the conditions
The conditions include what they are, when they occur, and how they occur. This indicates that certainty is not unconditional.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The philosophers were explaining where error can occur, not denying sensory certainty outright.

Refutation of the Deniers on Sense Perception
Objection and answer on sense perception reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard response to the objection that sense perception is unreliable due to occasional errors.

Core Idea
Occasional sensory error does not undermine certainty in error-free cases.

This brief passage addresses a fundamental challenge to empirical knowledge: if our senses can be mistaken, how can we ever be certain? The author presents the deniers' objection and gives a concise refutation that preserves the validity of sensory evidence when conditions for error are absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the deniers' claim: that the senses often err, implying sensory knowledge cannot be certain.
The term 'al-ḥissiyyāt' refers to sensory perceptions considered reliable.
2
Understand the answer
The answer is that error in some cases does not negate the possibility of certain knowledge in cases free from error. Focus on the phrase 'al-jazm al-muṭābiq' which means certain and correspondent cognition.
This defense preserves empirical knowledge by distinguishing conditions.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice that the objection assumes all sensory knowledge is uncertain because some instances err. The answer points out that this does not follow: error-free cases can still yield certainty.
This is a common logical fallacy (hasty generalization) that the answer corrects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المنكرون فمنهم من قدح في الحسيات بأن الحس قد يغلط كثيراً، والجواب أنه لا ينافي الجزم المطابق فيما لا غلط فيه)
He said: 'As for the deniers, among them are those who attacked sense perceptions by saying that the senses often err. The answer is that this does not contradict certain correspondent cognition in matters where there is no error.'
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection comes from those who deny the reliability of sensory knowledge (al-ḥissiyyāt). They argue that the senses frequently make mistakes, implying that no certain knowledge can be derived from them. The answer responds by clarifying that the occurrence of error in some cases does not negate the possibility of certain knowledge in cases that are free from error. The phrase 'al-jazm al-muṭābiq' refers to a judgment that is certain and corresponds to the actual state of affairs. Thus, as long as the conditions for error are absent, sensory knowledge can be certain. This defense is standard in Islamic epistemology to preserve the validity of empirical evidence.

Key Terms
الحسيات
الغلط
الجزم المطابق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any error in sense perception makes all sensory knowledge uncertain.
Correction: The passage shows that occasional error does not affect error-free cases, which can still yield certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the answer given to the objection that the senses often err?
A) The senses never err.
B) The intellect always corrects sensory errors.
C) Error does not contradict certainty in matters where there is no error.
D) Sensory knowledge is not needed for certainty.

Key Takeaway
Occasional sensory error does not invalidate all sensory knowledge; certainty is possible in error-free cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a small doubt or exception in your understanding of Islam, do you let it shake your entire faith, or do you treat it as an isolated case that doesn't invalidate the truth? Reflect on a specific instance and decide how you will handle similar doubts from now on.
?
How often do you rely on your senses to observe the signs of Allah in creation, yet allow the occasional uncertainty to rob you of certainty in His existence? What step will you take this week to strengthen your sensory conviction of His signs?
?
Think of a truth you know with certainty – maybe from the Quran or from your own experience. Have you ever abandoned that truth because someone pointed out a single exception or error? Will you let exceptions invalidate your certainty, or will you hold fast to what you know is true?

The Refutation of Sense as the Primary Judge
Commentator refutes the claim that sense is the primary judge and asserts reason as the ultimate arbiter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that reason, not sense, is the ultimate arbiter of truth, and that the possibility of error in sense does not make it the primary judge.

Core Idea
Sense perceives, but reason judges; error can occur in both, but reason remains the judge.

This passage from a commentary on al-Maqasid tackles a key epistemological claim: that sense perception is the primary judge of truth. The commentator refutes this by arguing that sense only perceives, while judgment belongs solely to reason. Even the possibility of error in sensory judgment does not elevate sense to a judge—rather, it underscores reason's role in distinguishing correct from incorrect. Let's examine the passage to see how this refutation is constructed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's overall flow.
Notice the commentator's use of 'حكم' and 'عقل' as central terms.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The Imam (al-Razi) argued that because sense can err, a higher judge is needed, making sense not the primary judge. The commentator's refutation takes a stronger stance.
Look for the phrase 'لا يكون الحس هو الحاكم الأول'.
3
Analyze the refutation
The commentator asserts that sense is not a judge at all; reason is the judge in everything. He clarifies that describing sensory things as certain or uncertain is only with respect to reason's judgment.
Note the phrase 'بل الحاكم في الكل هو العقل'.
4
Understand the nuance about error
The commentator argues that even reason's judgments in sensory matters can be erroneous, but this is not unique to sense—pure intelligibles can also contain error. This does not affect the role of reason as judge.
He says 'المعقولات الصرفة أيضاً قد يقع فيها الغلط'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by explaining that the Imam's discussion of causes of error is merely a warning for those who trust sense, not a requirement for trust itself. Trust in sense is based on pure reason without further proof.
Consider how this reinforces reason's primacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحسوسات، وأن أي أحكامها تكون يقينية، وأيها تكون غير يقينية ، صناعة المناظر كما جعلوا لبيان ذلك في المعقولات صناعة سوفسطيا، وما ذكر في تلخيص المحصل من أنه لا حكم للحس لأنه ليس من شأنه التأليف الحكمي ، بل الإدراك فقط ، وإنما الحكم للعقل، ليس رداً لكلام الإمام بالمناقشة في أن الحاكم هو الحس أو العقل بواسطته ، بل لما رتب عليه من المقصود حيث قال : فالمحسوس من حيث إنه محسوس لا يوصف بكونه يقينياً أو غير يقيني ، وإنما يوصف به من حيث مقارنته لحكم العقل، وحينئذ يصير المعنى أن أحكام العقل في المحسوسات ليست بيقينية لما قد يقع فيها من الغلط ، وهذا لا يختص بالمحسوسات، لأن المعقولات الصرفة أيضاً قد يقع فيها الغلط ، ولا تصح نسبته إلى الحكماء لتصريحهم بخلاف ذلك . نعم لما ذكر الإمام أنه ثبت بما ذكر من الشبه أن حكم الحس قد يكون غلطاً فلا بد من حاكم آخر فوقه يميز صوابه عن خطئه ، فلا يكون الحس هو الحاكم الأول، رده بأن الحس ليس بحاكم أصلاً ، بل الحاكم في الكل هو العقل ؛ وأما اشتغاله ببيان أسباب الغلط فيما أورده الإمام من الصور فقد اعترف بأنه تنبيه لمن يثق أو يعترف بالوقوف على الأوليات والمحسوسات ببيان التقصي عن مضايق مواضع الغلط ، ثم إحالة تصويب الصواب وتخطئة الخطأ إلى صريح العقل، من غير افتقار إلى دليل في الوثوق بالمحسوسات، ولا جواب عن شيء من الشكوك ، ولا تأمل في الأسباب وحصرها، وانتقائها ونحو ذلك،
The sensory things, and which of their judgments are certain and which are not certain, the art of investigating as they made for that in intelligibles the art of sophistry. And what was mentioned in Talkhis al-Muhassal that there is no judgment for sense because it is not its function to compose judgmental [propositions] but only perception, and judgment is only for reason—this is not a refutation of the Imam's speech by arguing that the judge is sense or reason through it, but rather for what was built upon it of the purpose where he said: The sensory qua sensory is not described as certain or uncertain, but is described as such from the perspective of its association with reason's judgment. Then the meaning becomes that reason's judgments in sensory things are not certain because error may occur in them, and this is not specific to sensory things because pure intelligibles may also have error, and it is not correct to attribute that to the philosophers due to their affirmation of the contrary. Yes, when the Imam mentioned that it is established by the mentioned doubts that the judgment of sense may be erroneous, so there must be another judge above it to distinguish its correct from its error, so sense is not the primary judge, he refuted it by [stating] that sense is not a judge at all, but the judge in everything is reason. As for his engagement in explaining the causes of error in the examples the Imam presented, he admitted that it is a warning for one who trusts or acknowledges standing upon primaries and sensibles by showing the scrutiny of the narrow places of error, then referring the correction of correct and the condemnation of error to pure reason, without needing a proof for trusting sense perceptions, and no response to any of the doubts, and no reflection on the causes and their enumeration, selection, and so forth.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by Imam al-Razi regarding the reliability of sense perception. The commentator begins by clarifying that the discussion about sensory judgments is not about sense itself but about the role of reason. He states that the claim that sense has no judgment because it only perceives is not a direct refutation of the Imam but addresses the implication. Then he explains that sensory things are described as certain or uncertain only in relation to reason's judgment. The commentator agrees that reason's judgments in sensory matters can be erroneous, but notes that this is not unique to sense—even pure intelligibles can have error. He then addresses the Imam's argument that because sense can err, a higher judge is needed. The refutation is that sense is not a judge at all; reason is the judge in everything. The commentator further argues that the Imam's discussion of causes of error is merely a warning for those who rely on sense, not a requirement for trust. The passage concludes that there is no need for proof or examination of doubts; trust in sense is based on pure reason without reflection on causes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sense is the primary judge, and the answer denies any error in sense perception.
Correction: The commentator does not deny error in sense; rather he says sense is not a judge at all, and both sense and reason can err, but reason is the ultimate judge.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the role of sense perception in judgment?
A) Sense is the primary judge in sensory matters.
B) Sense is not a judge at all; reason is the judge in everything.
C) Sense and reason both judge equally.
D) Sense judges, but its judgments are always unreliable.

Key Takeaway
Reason is the judge in all matters; sense perception, while potentially erroneous, does not serve as an independent judge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage teaches that reason itself can err. When you consider the most foundational beliefs you hold, how do you handle the realization that your own reasoning could be mistaken? Do you seek correction through study and reflection, or do you ignore this possibility? What is your ultimate criterion for truth beyond reason?
?
If sense is not a judge, then the physical evidence you see around you—your experiences, your environment—cannot independently verify truth. How does this affect your daily reliance on empirical evidence? In what areas of your life do you need to let reason, guided by divine revelation, be the final arbiter rather than your immediate perceptions?

Skeptical Doubts about Sense Perception and Their Refutation
Commentator enumerates skeptical doubts about sense perception and provides the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main skeptical doubts about sense perception raised in Islamic theology and how the commentator refutes them without denying the possibility of error.

Core Idea
Sense perception is reliable in clear cases despite occasional errors.

In this passage, the commentator summarizes the skeptical challenges to sense perception. He divides them into two categories: universals and particulars. He then provides a nuanced answer: error in some sensory judgments does not undermine certainty in many others, such as the heat of fire or the brightness of the sun.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two categories of doubt
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Pay attention to the opening phrase 'حاصل الشكوك' and the division into 'كليات' and 'جزئيات'.
The text is dense—pause after each category to absorb it.
2
Identify the examples of sensory illusions
The passage lists several optical illusions (e.g., grape in water, jar from a distance). List them and see how each demonstrates error in sensory particulars.
These examples are classic in Islamic philosophy.
3
Understand the answer: error in some cases does not negate certainty
Focus on the phrase 'أن غلطه في بعض الصور لا ينافي الجزم المطابق في كثير من الصور'. This is the core refutation.
The examples of sun and fire are given as clear cases where error is impossible.
4
Reflect on the final point from al-Mawaqif
The commentator quotes al-Mawaqif to clarify that the doubt only prevents judging solely based on sense, not that all sensory judgments are uncertain.
This nuance preserves trust in reason while acknowledging the role of sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الشكوك أنه لا وثوق على حكم الحس، أما في الكليات فلأنه لا يحيط بها كيف وهي لا تقتصر على الأفراد المحققة . وأما في الجزئيات فلأنه كثير ما يكون حكمه فيها غلطاً، بأن يقع الحكم في المحسوسات على خلاف ما هو عليه ، فإنا نرى الصغير كبيراً أو بالعكس والواحد كثيراً أو بالعكس، والساكن متحركاً إلى غير ذلك، كما نرى العنبة في الماء كالأجاصة، والجرة من بعيد كالكوز والقمر في الماء قمرين والألوان المختلفة في الخطوط المخرجة من مركز الرحى إلى محيطها عند إدارتها لوناً واحداً ممتزجاً من الكل، ويرى من في السفينة ساكنة وهي متحركة والشط متحركاً وهو ساكن، إلى غير ذلك. والجواب : أن غلطه في بعض الصور لا ينافي الجزم المطابق في كثير من الصور كما في الحكم بأن الشمس مضيئة والنار حارة، إذ العقل قاطع بأنه لا غلط هناك من غير افتقار إلى نظر، وإن كان ذلك بمعونة أمور لا تعلم على التفصيل وهذا ما قاله في المواقف : إن مقتضى ما ذكر من الشبه أن لا يجزم العقل بأحكام المحسوسات لمجرد الحس لا أن لا يوثق بجزمه بما جزم به وكونه محتملاً، أي ولا أن يكون كل ما جزم به العقل من أحكام المحسوسات محتملاً أي بصدد الاحتمال بناء على عدم الوثوق بما وقع فيه من الجزم.
The summary of the doubts is that there is no reliance on the judgment of sense: as for universals, because it does not encompass them—how can it, since they are not restricted to actual individuals? And as for particulars, because often its judgment in them is erroneous, such that judgment occurs in sensory things contrary to how they are. For we see the small as large or vice versa, the one as many or vice versa, the stationary as moving, and so on, such as we see a grape in water like a pear, a jar from a distance like a pitcher, the moon in water as two moons, and the different colors in lines drawn from the center of a millstone to its circumference when rotating as a single color blended from all, and one on a ship sees the ship as stationary while it is moving and the shore moving while it is stationary, and so on. The answer: that its error in some cases does not contradict certainty that corresponds [to reality] in many cases, as in the judgment that the sun is bright and fire is hot, for reason is certain that there is no error there without needing reflection, even if that is with the aid of things not known in detail. And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: that the implication of the mentioned doubts is that reason should not judge sensory judgments solely on the basis of sense, not that one should not trust its certainty in what it has judged and its being possible; i.e., nor that every judgment reason has made about sensory things is subject to possibility, based on not trusting the certainty that has occurred in it.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the summary of skeptical doubts about sense perception and the answer. It begins by stating the doubt: there is no reliance on sense judgments. The doubt is divided into two categories. First, regarding universals (al-kulliyat): sense cannot encompass them because they are not limited to actual individuals. Second, regarding particulars (al-juz'iyat): sense often errs, with examples such as seeing a small object as large, one as many, stationary as moving, and various optical illusions like a grape in water appearing as a pear, a jar from a distance as a pitcher, the moon's reflection as two moons, colors blending in a rotating disk, and the illusion of motion on a ship. The answer (al-jawab) is that error in some cases does not negate certainty in many cases, as in the clear judgments that the sun is bright and fire is hot. Reason is certain without needing reflection, even if aided by unknown details. The passage quotes al-Mawaqif to clarify that the doubts only show that reason should not judge solely based on sense, not that its judgments are always uncertain. The final point is that it is not the case that every sensory judgment of reason is subject to possibility due to lack of trust in the certainty that has occurred.

Key Terms
حاصل الشكوك
كليات
جزئيات
جزم
احتمال
غلط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the answer denies all error in sense perception.
Correction: The answer only denies that error negates certainty in clear cases; it acknowledges that error occurs in some instances.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the skeptical doubts about sense perception?
A) All sense judgments are unreliable due to optical illusions.
B) Error in some sensory judgments proves that reason cannot be trusted.
C) Error in some cases does not contradict certainty in many other clear cases.
D) The only reliable judgments are those based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The answer to skeptical doubts about sense perception is that occasional errors do not undermine the certainty we have in clear sensory judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The answer in al-Mawaqif says doubts only show reason should not judge solely by sense, not that certainty is impossible. Is there a conviction about Allah or His attributes that you hesitate to affirm with full certainty because of intellectual doubts? What would it take to trust the clear evidence you have?
