Sensory Skepticism Refuted: The Reliability of Sense Certainty
Author presents and refutes skeptic arguments against sense certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the certainty of sense perception against skeptical arguments, showing that error in some instances does not undermine reason's certainty in clear cases.

Core Idea
Sensory errors prove sense is fallible, not that reason cannot be certain.

This lesson addresses a classic skeptical challenge: if our senses sometimes deceive us, can we ever be certain about what we perceive? The author presents the strongest skeptical objections and then offers a concise but powerful response, distinguishing between uncritical sense reports and the certain judgments of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Then read the literal translation carefully.
Note the examples of sensory illusions.
2
Identify the skeptic's two-pronged attack
The skeptic argues against sense certainty for both universals and particulars. Find these two lines and the examples given for particulars.
The examples are crucial to understanding the claim.
3
Focus on the author's response
Read the answer section carefully. Notice how the author does not deny sensory errors but argues they do not negate certainty in clear cases like the sun's luminosity.
The quote from al-Mawaqif clarifies the scope of the skeptic's objection.
4
Reflect on the philosophical principle
Consider the implication: reason can be certain even when assisted by unknown factors. How does this defend sense certainty without requiring omniscience?
This is a key move in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصواب وتخطئة الخطأ إلى صريح العقل، من غير افتقار إلى دليل في الوثوق بالمحسوسات، ولا جواب عن شيء من الشكوك ، ولا تأمل في الأسباب وحصرها، وانتقائها ونحو ذلك، وحاصل الشكوك أنه لا وثوق على حكم الحس، أما في الكليات (۱) فلأنه لا يحيط بها كيف وهي لا تقتصر على الأفراد المحققة . وأما في الجزئيات فلأنه (٢) كثير ما يكون حكمه فيها غلطاً، بأن يقع الحكم في المحسوسات على خلاف ما هو عليه ، فإنا نرى الصغير كبيراً أو بالعكس(۳) والواحد كثيراً أو بالعكس، والساكن متحركاً إلى غير ذلك، كما نرى العنبة في الماء كالأجاصة، والجرة من بعيد كالكوز والقمر في الماء ،قمرين والألوان المختلفة في الخطوط المخرجة من مركز الرحى إلى محيطها عند إدارتها لوناً واحداً ممتزجاً من الكل، ويرى(٤) من في السفينة ساكنة وهي متحركة والشط متحركاً وهو ساكن، إلى غير ذلك.
والجواب : أن غلطه في بعض الصور لا ينافي الجزم المطابق في كثير من الصور كما في الحكم بأن الشمس مضيئة والنار حارة، إذ العقل قاطع بأنه لا غلط هناك من غير افتقار إلى نظر، وإن كان ذلك بمعونة أمور لا تعلم على وجه التفصيل وهذا ما قاله في المواقف : إن مقتضى ما ذكر من الشبه أن لا يجزم العقل بأحكام المحسوسات لمجرد الحس (٦) لا أن لا يوثق بجزمه بما جزم به وكونه محتملاً، أي ولا أن يكون كل ما جزم به العقل من أحكام المحسوسات محتملاً أي بصدد الاحتمال بناء على عدم الوثوق بما وقع فيه من الجزم.
(۱) الكلي : عند المنطقيين، هو الشامل لجميع الأفراد الداخلين في صنف معين، أو هو المفهوم الذي لا يمنع تصوره من أن يشتمل فيه كثيرون . قال ابن سينا اللفظ المفرد الكلي هو الذي يدل على كثيرين في الوجود كالإنسان أو كثيرين في جواز التوهم : كالشمس، وبالجملة الكلي : هو اللفظ الذي لا يمنع مفهومه أن يشترك في معناه كثيرون فإن منع من ذلك شيء فهو غير نفس مفهومه . (راجع النجاة ص (۸ والكليات الخمس هي الجنس والنوع والفصل. والخاصة، والعرض العام . (۲) في (ب) فلأن . (۳) في (ب) وبالعكس بدون الهمزة. (٤) في (ب) ونرى. (٥) في (ب) أنه . (٦) في (أ) ألا بزيادة همزة .
فقوله، وكونه محتملا مرفوع معطوف على أن لا وثوق (۱) لا مجرور معطوف على جزمه، إذ ليس فيه كثير معنى . (۱) في (أ) لا يوثق .
The truth and labeling error as error [are referred] to plain reason, without needing a proof in trusting sense-perceptions, nor any response to any of the doubts, nor reflection on causes and their enumeration, their selection, and so on. The summary of the doubts is that there is no trust in the judgment of sense: as for universals (1), because it does not encompass them, how could it, since they are not restricted to actual individuals. And as for particulars, because its judgment in them is often mistaken, in that the judgment about sense-perceptibles occurs contrary to what they are. For we see the small as large or vice versa (3), the one as many or vice versa, the stationary as moving, and so forth; as we see the grape in water like a pear, the jar from afar like a small pot, the moon in water as two moons, and the different colors on the lines drawn from the center of the millstone to its circumference when it is spinning as one color mixed from all; and [we see] the one in the ship as stationary while it is moving, and the shore as moving while it is stationary, and so forth.
And the answer: that its error in some instances does not contradict the certain correspondence [to reality] in many instances, as in the judgment that the sun is luminous and fire is hot, for reason is certain that there is no error there without needing reflection, even if that is with the aid of matters not known in detail. And this is what he said in al-Mawaqif: 'What is required from what has been mentioned of the doubts is that reason should not make a judgment about sense-perceptions merely by sense, not that it should not trust its own judgment when it has judged, and [not] that it is possible [to be wrong]' — i.e., nor that every judgment reason makes about sense-perceptibles is possible [to be wrong], i.e., subject to possibility, based on the lack of trust in the certainty that occurs.
(1) al-kulli: according to the logicians, it is what encompasses all individuals falling within a certain kind, or the concept whose conception does not prevent many from being included in it. Ibn Sina said: the single universal term is that which indicates many in existence like 'man', or many in permissible supposition like 'sun'; in short, the universal is the term whose concept does not prevent many from sharing in its meaning, for if something prevents that, it is not its own concept. (Refer to al-Najat p. 8; the five universals are genus, species, difference, property, and common accident.) (2) In copy (b): 'fa-inna'. (3) In copy (b): 'wa-bi-al-'aks' without hamza. (4) In copy (b): 'wa-nara'. (5) In copy (b): 'annahu'. (6) In copy (a): 'illa' with an extra hamza.
His saying 'and that it is possible' is in the nominative, coordinated with 'that there is no trust' — not in the genitive, coordinated with 'its judgment', for there is not much meaning in it. (1) In copy (a): 'la yuthaq'.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is an argument-and-response structured around skepticism regarding sense perception. The author first states the correct method: truth and error are determined by plain reason without needing external proof for sense-perceptions. He then summarizes the skeptic's doubts: sense cannot encompass universals because they are not limited to actual individuals, and for particulars, sense often errs, as illustrated by numerous examples of visual illusions (size, number, motion, color). The response follows: error in some instances does not negate certainty in many clear cases, such as the sun being luminous or fire hot; reason is certain without requiring detailed knowledge of the assisting conditions. The author then quotes al-Mawaqif to clarify that the skeptic's argument only prevents judgment based solely on sense, not the certainty of reason's judgments. Finally, a grammatical note explains the parsing of the phrase 'wa-kawnihi muhtamala'.

Key Terms
المحسوسات
الشكوك
الكليات
الجزئيات
الجزم
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory illusions show that sense perception can never be trusted.
Correction: The author argues that error in some instances does not negate certainty in many clear cases; reason can be certain about some sense perceptions without requiring detailed investigation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does sensory error in some cases not invalidate all sense certainty?
A) Because reason has a separate faculty that never errs
B) Because in many clear cases there is no error, and reason is certain without detailed investigation
C) Because sense is always reliable and the examples are false
D) Because the skeptic's argument is logically invalid

Key Takeaway
Sensory errors are limited and do not undermine reason's ability to be certain about clear sense perceptions, such as the sun's brightness and fire's heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced a doubt about something clear in your faith because of a past error or confusion? How does this lesson's distinction between error in some cases and certainty in clear cases challenge you to trust your reason more confidently in matters where evidence is plain?
?
This lesson insists that reason can be certain without knowing every detail of how the perception works. Are there aspects of your belief in Allah or the truth of Islam that you have hesitated to affirm because you couldn't explain every 'how'? What would it mean for you to embrace the certainty reason affords despite incomplete understanding?
?
The skeptic's examples of optical illusions are vivid and unsettling. Yet the author points to the sun's light and fire's heat as undeniable. In your own life, what 'suns' and 'fires' has Allah placed before you—clear signs you cannot deny? Will you let the scattered illusions of doubt steal your certainty, or will you hold fast to what is luminous and undeniable?

Critique of the Principle of Non-Contradiction
Author presents and answers the objection against the most evident self-evident truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the most self-evident truth (the principle of non-contradiction) against the objection that it depends on subtle concepts, and why critiquing a branch does not undermine the root.

Core Idea
Challenging the principle of non-contradiction does not destroy certainty; it is a derivative issue.

Sanusi quotes a critic who argues that even the most evident truth, the principle of non-contradiction, depends on understanding existence and non-existence and thus cannot be unquestionable. The author then provides his own analysis, showing that self-evident truths are branches of sense experience, and attacking a branch does not necessarily invalidate the root. This lesson unpacks the objection and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the critic's argument: the most evident self-evident truth depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, etc. Note the claim that it does not engender real doubt.
The quoted response is brief.
2
Study the author's answer
The author says self-evident truths are branches of sense experience. Attacking a branch does not necessarily attack the root unless the branch is inherently necessary to the root.
This is the key defense.
3
Reflect on the principle's status
Consider why the principle of non-contradiction is considered most evident and highest, and how the author's argument preserves its certainty despite the critique.
The author does not abandon the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (ومنهم من قدح في البديهيات بأن أجلاها وأعلاها الشيء إما أن يكون ، وإما أن لا يكون (۲) ، وهو يتوقف على تصور الوجود (۳) والعدم )) وتحقيق معنى الوضع والحمل، ودفع شبهاتهما، وفيها أفكار دقيقة، والجواب أنها لا تورث شكاً فإن شئناً أعرضنا وإن شئتاً نبهنا) (ه) أقول (۹) : إنها فرع الحسيات لأن الإنسان إنما يتنبه للبديهيات بعد الإحساس بالجزئيات والتنبه لما بينهما من المشاركات والمباينات. ولا يلزم من القدح في الفرع القدح في الأصل، وإنما يلزم لو كان الفرع لازماً له نظراً إلى ذاته، ووجه القدح أن أجلى التصديقات ليس (1) البديهية وأعلاها قولنا النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، يعني أن الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون وهذا غير موثوق به، أما كونه أجلى فجلى، وأما كونه أعلى أي أسبق فلتوقف الكل عليه، واستنادها إليه مثلاً يلاحظ في قولنا الكل الاستقرار، (۲) سقط من (ب) لفظ (يكون). (۳) الوجود عند الفلاسفة : مقابل للماهية. لأن الماهية هي الطبيعة المعقولة للشيء. والوجود هو التحقق الفعلي له وكون له وكون الشيء حاصلا في التجربة غير كونه ذا طبيعة معقولة . ومن الفلاسفة من يقول : إن وجود الشيء زائد على ماهيته كابن سينا، ومنهم من يقول : إن وجود الشيء عين ماهيته كوجود الإنسان. والوجود ينقسم إلى وجود خارجي، ووجود ذهني. فالخارجي عبارة عن كون الشيء في الأعيان وهو الوجود المادي، والوجود الذهني . عبارة عن كون الشيء في الأذهان، وهو الوجود العقلي أو المنطقي . (٤) العدم ضد الوجود وهو مطلق أو إضافي. فالعدم المطلق هو الذي لا يضاف إلى شيء. والعدم الإضافي، أو المقيد، هو المضاف إلى شيء. كقولنا عدم الأمن، عدم وعدم التأثر. قال ابن سينا البالغ في النقص غايته فهو المنتهى إلى مطلق العدم فبالأحرى أن يطلق عليه معنى العدم المطلق . (الإشارات ٦٩ - ٧٠) . (٥) سقط من (أ) لفظ (أقول) . (٦) سقط من (أ) لفظ (ليس).
He said: 'And among them is one who criticized the self-evident [truths] by [saying that] the most evident and highest of them is that a thing either is or is not (2), and it depends on conceiving existence (3) and non-existence (4), and realizing the meaning of position and predication, and refuting their doubts, and in them are subtle thoughts. And the answer: they do not engender doubt; if we wish we can turn away, and if we wish we can draw attention.' (5) I say (9): They are branches of the sense-perceptibles, for man only becomes attentive to self-evident truths after sensing particulars and noticing the commonalities and differences between them. And it does not follow from criticizing the branch to criticize the root; it would only follow if the branch were necessary to it in terms of its essence. And the manner of the criticism is that the most evident of judgments is not (1) the self-evident one, and the highest of them is our statement 'negation and affirmation do not concur and do not both disappear', meaning that a thing either is or is not, and this is not reliable. As for its being most evident, that is evident; and as for its being highest, i.e., prior, [that is] because everything depends on it, and its being dependent on it, for example, is observed in our statement 'the whole ...' (2) In copy (b): the word 'yakun' is missing. (3) Existence according to the philosophers: opposite of quiddity. Because quiddity is the intelligible nature of a thing, and existence is its actual realization, and the thing's being present in experience is other than its being of an intelligible nature. Some philosophers say that the existence of a thing is additional to its quiddity, like Ibn Sina; others say that the existence of a thing is identical to its quiddity, like the existence of man. Existence is divided into external existence and mental existence. External is the thing being in external objects, which is material existence; mental existence is the thing being in minds, which is intellectual or logical existence. (4) Non-existence is opposite of existence, and it is absolute or relative. Absolute non-existence is that which is not attributed to anything. Relative or restricted non-existence is that which is attributed to something, like 'absence of security', 'absence of impression'. Ibn Sina said: the one who reaches the utmost deficiency is the one who ends at absolute non-existence, so it is more appropriate to apply the meaning of absolute non-existence to him. (al-Isharat 69-70). (5) In copy (a): the word 'aqul' is missing. (6) In copy (a): the word 'laysa' is missing.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the most evident self-evident truth, the principle of non-contradiction. The author quotes a critic who argues that even this principle depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, and on resolving subtle issues about predication, thus it is not unquestionable. The quoted response claims it does not cause real doubt. The author then adds his own explanation: self-evident truths are branches of sense experience, so criticizing the branch does not necessarily invalidate the root unless the branch is inherently necessary to the root. He then explains the critic's point further: the most evident judgment is not this self-evident one; the highest is the statement that negation and affirmation cannot both be true or both false, but even this is considered unreliable. The author notes its evidentness and priority are clear because everything depends on it, but he does not fully resolve the criticism; the text is cut off.

Correction: The author shows that self-evident truths are branches of sense experience; critiquing a branch does not necessarily invalidate the root.

B) He argues that self-evident truths are branches of sense perception, so critiquing the branch does not invalidate the root

Key Takeaway

Answering the Objection to Self-Evident Truths
Al-Taftazani refutes an objection against self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection against self-evident truths fails according to al-Taftazani, and how the mind's intuition provides certainty without circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are known by intuition, not dependent on subtle reasoning.

Al-Taftazani addresses a philosophical objection that challenges the certainty of self-evident truths, especially the principle of non-contradiction. The objection argues that these truths require complex reasoning about existence and non-existence, leading to a vicious circle. Al-Taftazani defends the immediate certainty of these truths, grounding them in the innate intuition of the mind. This lesson walks through the objection and the author's response, clarifying why philosophical doubts do not undermine foundational knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: a grammatical remark, then the objection, then the author's commentary and answer.
The first sentence is a grammatical note about a prior statement; the main content begins with 'قال: (ومنهم من قدح في البديهيات...'.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the core objection: the most self-evident judgment ('a thing either is or is not') is unreliable because certainty depends on three things: conceiving existence and non-existence, verifying subject-predicate relation, and refuting doubts. This leads to circularity or loss of certainty.
The objection claims that if these are resolved through reasoning, the proposition becomes theoretical, contradicting its self-evident nature.
3
Analyze the author's answer
Al-Taftazani responds by asserting that the mind's intuition (badiha al-'aql) is decisive about self-evident truths without any reflection or inference. Doubts do not affect this innate certainty. He also offers two strategies for dealing with skeptics: ignore them or alert them.
The answer emphasizes that self-evident truths are not derived from sensory or rational inferences; they are immediate.
4
Consider the specific doubt
The passage ends with a specific doubt: the judgment depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, but absolute non-existence cannot be established without contradiction. Note how this doubt attempts to undermine the very concepts needed for the principle.
The passage continues on the next page, but this lesson covers up to this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقوله، وكونه محتملا مرفوع معطوف على أن لا وثوق لا مجرور معطوف على جزمه، إذ ليس فيه كثير معنى .
قال: (ومنهم من قدح في البديهيات بأن أجلاها وأعلاها الشيء إما أن يكون، وإما أن لا يكون، وهو يتوقف على تصور الوجود والعدم) وتحقيق معنى الوضع والحمل، ودفع شبهاتهما، وفيها أفكار دقيقة، والجواب أنها لا تورث شكاً فإن شئناً أعرضنا وإن شئتاً نبهنا
أقول: إنها فرع الحسيات لأن الإنسان إنما يتنبه للبديهيات بعد الإحساس بالجزئيات والتنبه لما بينهما من المشاركات والمباينات.
ولا يلزم من القدح في الفرع القدح في الأصل، وإنما يلزم لو كان الفرع لازماً له نظراً إلى ذاته، ووجه القدح أن أجلى التصديقات ليس البديهية وأعلاها قولنا النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، يعني أن الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون وهذا غير موثوق به، أما كونه أجلى فجلى، وأما كونه أعلى أي أسبق فلتوقف الكل عليه، واستنادها إليه مثلاً يلاحظ في قولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء، أنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان الجزء الآخر كائناً وليس بكائن، وفي قولنا: الجسم الواحد لا يكون في آن واحد في مكانين، أنه لو وجد فيهما لكان الواحد اثنين، فيكون أحد المثلين كائناً وليس بكائن، وعلى هذا القياس، وأما عدم الوثوق فلأن العلم بحقيقة هذه القضية وقطعيتها يتوقف على تصور الوجود والعدم أعني الكون واللاكون وعلى تحقيق معنى كون الشيء موضوعاً وكونه محمولاً، وعلى دفع الشبهات التي تورد على الأمرين، وهذه الأمور الثلاثة إنما تتبين بأنظار دقيقة فإن تمت الأنظار وحصلت المطالب ويتوقف لا محالة على أحقية هذه القضية لكونها أول الأوائل لزم الدور وكون الشيء نظرياً على تقدير كونه ضرورياً وهو محال، وإن بقي شيء منها في حيز الإبهام لم يحصل الجزم بالقضية، وهو المرام، والجواب أن بديهة العقل جازمة بها وبحقيقتها من غير نظر واستدلال في تحقيق النسبة ولا في دفع الشبهة، وما يورد من الشكوك لا يورث قدحاً في ذلك الجزم، ولا يمكن دفعه بالنسبة إلى لا يعترف بالبديهيات فإن شئنا أعرضنا عنه وإن شئنا نبهناه عسى أن يعترف أو يحصل له استعداد النظر واستحقاق المباحثة فمن الشبه أن هذا التصديق يتوقف على تصور الوجود والعدم وتميزهما، وهذا يقتضي الثبوت ولو في الذهن، وثبوت العدم المطلق تناقض ثم لا بد من إمكان سلب العدم المطلق ليتحقق الوجود في الجملة فيكون هذا السلب قسماً من العدم
His statement: 'and its being possible' is nominative, coordinated with 'that there is no trust' – not genitive coordinated with 'its decisiveness', since there is no meaning in it.
He said: 'Among them are those who objected to self-evident truths by [saying]: the most clear and highest of them is that a thing either is or is not, and it depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, and verifying the meaning of subject and predicate, and refuting their doubts, and there are subtle thoughts in them. And the answer is that they do not produce doubt; if we wish we turn away, and if we wish we alert.'
I say: They are branches of sensory things, because a person only becomes aware of self-evident truths after sensation of particulars and after attending to the commonalities and differences between them.
And it does not follow from objecting to the branch to object to the root; it would follow only if the branch were necessary to it in its essence. And the aspect of the objection is that the most clear of judgments is not the self-evident, and the highest of them is our statement: 'Negation and affirmation do not combine nor are they removed', meaning that a thing either is or is not, and this is not reliable. As for its being the most clear, it is clear; as for its being the highest, i.e. most prior, because everything depends on it and is based on it. For example, it is noticed in our statement: 'The whole is greater than the part' – that if it were not so, the other part would be existent and not existent. And in our statement: 'One body cannot be in two places at one time' – that if it existed in both, the one would be two, so one of the two similars would be existent and not existent. And so on. And as for the lack of reliability, because knowledge of the reality and certainty of this proposition depends on conceiving existence and non-existence – meaning existence and non-existence – and on verifying the meaning of a thing being a subject and being a predicate, and on refuting the doubts brought up concerning these two matters. And these three matters are only made clear by subtle reflections; if the reflections are completed and the objects attained – and it necessarily depends on the truth of this proposition because it is the first of first principles – then circularity follows, and the thing being theoretical on the assumption of its being necessary – which is impossible. And if anything of them remains in the realm of obscurity, then decisiveness about the proposition is not attained – and that is the intent. And the answer is that the intuition of the mind is decisive about it and about its reality without reflection or inference in verifying the relation or in refuting the doubt. And what is brought of doubts does not produce objection to that decisiveness, and it cannot be repelled with respect to one who does not acknowledge self-evident truths; if we wish we turn away from him, and if we wish we alert him, that he might acknowledge or acquire preparedness for reflection and worthiness of discussion. Among the doubts is that this judgment depends on conceiving existence and non-existence and distinguishing them, and this necessitates establishment even in the mind, and the establishment of absolute non-existence is a contradiction. Then there must be possibility of negating absolute non-existence so that existence may be realized in general, so this negation would be a division of non-existence.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the certainty of self-evident truths (al-badihiyyat), specifically the principle of non-contradiction (al-nafi wa al-ithbat la yajtami'an wa la yartafi'an), followed by the author's refutation. The structure: first a grammatical remark about a prior statement, then the objection as a quote, then the author's commentary ('I say') explaining that self-evident truths are based on sensory experience and that attacking the branch does not necessarily attack the root. The objection argues that the most self-evident judgment ('a thing either is or is not') is unreliable because its certainty depends on three things: conceiving existence and non-existence, verifying the subject-predicate relation, and refuting doubts. If these are settled only through subtle reasoning, circularity (al-dawr) results, or the proposition becomes theoretical while being assumed necessary, which is absurd. If any remains obscure, certainty fails. The author's answer: the mind's intuition (badiha al-'aql) is decisive without reflection; doubts do not undermine that innate certainty. Regarding someone who does not accept self-evidences, he proposes either ignoring them or alerting them in hope of recognition. He then addresses a specific doubt: the judgment depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, which requires a mental existence of non-existence, but absolute non-existence cannot be established – leading to a paradox that the text begins to unpack but is cut off.

بديهة العقل

Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that the mind's intuition provides immediate certainty without need for reflection or inference.

The Law of Non-Contradiction is Self-Evident
Explains the problem of certainty in the law of non-contradiction and answers with its self-evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the law of non-contradiction is considered self-evident in Islamic theology and how it is defended against doubts.

Core Idea
The law of non-contradiction is known by rational intuition, not by theoretical proof.

This passage addresses a fundamental problem: if the law of non-contradiction requires proof, it leads to circularity. The author argues that it is self-evident, grounded in immediate rational intuition. Let's examine his reasoning and see how he handles doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the problem
The author first gives an example of the law (a body cannot be in two places at once) and then raises a problem: proving the law seems to require prior knowledge, leading to circularity.
Notice the example: if a body were in two places, the one would be two, implying something both existent and non-existent.
2
Understand the three dependencies for certainty
Certainty depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, understanding subject and predicate, and refuting doubts. These require precise investigations.
If these investigations rely on the law itself, circularity arises; if any vagueness remains, certainty is lost.
3
Find the author's answer
The author resolves by appealing to rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql), which is decisive about the law without any investigation.
This is the key point: the law is self-evident, not derived from theoretical reasoning.
4
Reflect on the handling of doubts
Doubts do not undermine the decisiveness of rational intuition. For those who reject self-evident truths, one can either disregard them or gently guide them.
This shows the author's practical approach to skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعظم من الجزء، أنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان الجزء الآخر كائناً وليس بكائن، وفي قولنا : الجسم الواحد لا يكون في آن واحد في مكانين، أنه لو وجد فيهما لكان الواحد اثنين، فيكون أحد المثلين كائناً وليس بكائن، وعلى هذا القياس، وأما عدم الوثوق فلأن العلم بحقيقة هذه القضية وقطعيتها يتوقف على تصور الوجود والعدم أعني الكون واللاكون وعلى تحقيق معنى كون الشيء موضوعاً وكونه محمولاً ، وعلى دفع الشبهات التي تورد على الأمرين، وهذه الأمور الثلاثة إنما تتبين بأنظار دقيقة فإن تمت الأنظار وحصلت المطالب ويتوقف لا محالة على أحقية هذه القضية لكونها أول الأوائل لزم الدور وكون الشيء نظرياً على تقدير كونه ضرورياً وهو محال، وإن بقي شيء منها في حيز الإبهام لم يحصل الجزم بالقضية، وهو المرام، والجواب أن بديهة العقل جازمة بها وبحقيقتها من غير نظر واستدلال في تحقيق النسبة ولا في دفع الشبهة، وما يورد من الشكوك لا يورث قدحاً في ذلك الجزم، ولا يمكن دفعه بالنسبة إلى لا يعترف بالبديهيات فإن شئنا أعرضنا عنه وإن شئنا نبهناه عسى أن يعترف أو يحصل له استعداد النظر واستحقاق المباحثة
Greater than the part: that if it were not so, then the other part would be existent and not existent. And in our statement: 'A single body cannot be in two places at one time', that if it existed in both, then the one would be two, so one of the two would be existent and not existent. And analogously. As for lack of certainty, knowledge of the reality of this proposition and its apodicticity depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, i.e., being and not-being, and on investigating the meaning of something being a subject and being a predicate, and on refuting the doubts that are raised concerning the two matters. These three matters are only clarified by precise investigations. If the investigations are completed and the goals are attained, but necessarily depend on [the proposition] being the first of first principles, then there is circularity, and the thing would be theoretical on the assumption of its being necessary, which is impossible. If any of it remains in the realm of vagueness, certainty about the proposition is not attained, which is the aim. The answer: that the rational intuition is decisive about it and about its reality without investigation and reasoning in investigating the relation or in refuting the doubt. What is raised of doubts does not cause any defect in that decisiveness, and it cannot be refuted for one who does not acknowledge self-evident truths. If we wish, we disregard him; if we wish, we alert him, perhaps he will acknowledge or acquire readiness for investigation and worthiness of discussion.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-answer concerning the law of non-contradiction. The author first illustrates the law with an example: a body cannot be in two places at once because that would entail something being both existent and non-existent. He then raises a problem: certainty in this proposition depends on three things—conceiving existence and non-existence, understanding subject and predicate, and refuting doubts. These require subtle investigations, and if they rely on the proposition being first, circularity arises; if any vagueness remains, certainty is lost. The answer is that rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql) is decisive about the proposition and its reality without any investigation. Doubts do not undermine this decisiveness, and for one who rejects self-evident truths, one can either ignore or attempt to guide him to readiness for reasoning. The author thus upholds the principle as self-evident.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
قضية

لاكون

محمول
دور
نظر
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the law of non-contradiction requires prior theoretical proof.
Correction: The author stresses it is self-evident, known directly by rational intuition without investigation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for certainty in the law of non-contradiction?
A) Precise logical investigations
B) Rational intuition without investigation
C) Refutation of all doubts
D) Recognition of the subject-predicate relation

Key Takeaway
The law of non-contradiction is a self-evident principle known by rational intuition, not dependent on theoretical proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever known something to be true in your heart—like the wrongness of a sin—yet pretended to doubt it so you could continue in it? What would it mean to finally submit to that self-evident truth?
?
The law of non-contradiction tells us that something cannot be both true and false at the same time. But in your own life, are you living as if two contradictory things can both be true—like claiming to trust Allah while refusing to obey His commands? Where is the contradiction, and what will you do about it today?
?
If rational intuition is decisive about the most basic truths, why do you still hesitate to act on what you already know is right? What false 'doubt' are you hiding behind to avoid the demand that truth places on your life?

Resolving the Doubt on Conceiving Non-Existence
Presents a doubt and its answer on existence and non-existence concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a doubt about the conceivability of absolute non-existence is resolved by distinguishing between essential and mental existence.

Core Idea
A concept can be mentally affirmed while essentially non-existent—no contradiction.

The passage presents a doubt: if knowledge depends on concepts like existence and non-existence, then absolute non-existence must be conceived, yet affirming it seems contradictory. The author answers by clarifying the distinction between essential and mental affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the opening of the passage where the doubt is presented. Note that it claims assent depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, and that this requires affirming absolute non-existence in the mind, which is contradictory.
Pay attention to the terms 'absolute non-existence' and 'relative non-existence'.
2
Identify the structure of the doubt
The doubt has two parts: (1) affirming absolute non-existence is a contradiction, and (2) the negation of absolute non-existence needed for existence becomes part of non-existence. Understand each part.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The author explains that there is no impossibility in something being non-existent in essence but existent in the mind. Also, being a part of non-existence (relative non-existence) is not the same as absolute non-existence. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
This distinction is key to avoiding the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن الشبه أن هذا التصديق يتوقف على تصور الوجود والعدم وتميزهما ( )، وهذا يقتضي الثبوت ولو في الذهن، وثبوت العدم المطلق تناقض ثم لا بد من إمكان سلب العدم المطلق ليتحقق الوجود في الجملة فيكون هذا السلب قسماً من العدم المطلق [؟] لكونه عدماً مضافاً وقسيماً له، لكونه رفعاً له وسلباً. والجواب : أنه لا استحالة في كون المعنى لا ثابتاً من حيث الذات والمفهوم وثابتاً من حيث الحصول في العقل، ولا في كونه قسماً من العدم من حيث كونه عدماً مضافاً وقسيماً له من حيث المفهوم. وسيجيء لهذا زيادة تحقيق في بحث العدم .
Among the doubts is that this assent depends on conceiving existence and non-existence and distinguishing them ( ), and this necessitates affirmation, even if in the mind; and the affirmation of absolute non-existence is a contradiction. Then it is necessary to have the possibility of negating absolute non-existence so that existence is realized in some way; so this negation would be a part of absolute non-existence, because it is a relative non-existence and a counterpart to it, because it is a removal and negation of it. The answer: that there is no impossibility in a meaning being not established in terms of essence and concept while established in terms of occurrence in the mind, nor in it being a part of non-existence in terms of it being a relative non-existence and a counterpart to it in terms of concept. Further detail will come in the discussion of non-existence.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a doubt-and-answer pair. The doubt claims that the law of non-contradiction depends on conceiving existence and non-existence, which requires the concept of absolute non-existence to be affirmed in the mind, yet affirming absolute non-existence is contradictory. Moreover, negating absolute non-existence is needed for existence, but that negation itself becomes a part of non-existence. The answer clarifies that a concept can be not established in essence yet established in the mind without contradiction. Also, being a part of non-existence by being a relative non-existence is different from absolute non-existence, and this is acceptable conceptually. The author promises further detail later.

Resolving the Paradox of Absolute Negation
Taftazani answers an objection about defining 'al-matluq' as relative non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction in defining absolute negation as both a relative non-existence and a counterpart to existence, by distinguishing between essence/concept and mental existence.

Core Idea
A single thing can be non-existent in reality yet existent in the mind—the key is distinguishing aspects.

This short passage presents a subtle objection and its answer. The objection claims that defining 'al-matluq' as a relative non-existence is problematic because it would make it both a division of non-existence and a counterpart to existence at the same time. Taftazani's answer reveals a crucial distinction: something can be 'not fixed' in its essence and concept (no external reality) while being 'fixed' as a mental concept. This distinction resolves the surface contradiction and prepares for deeper discussions on non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection defines al-matluq as a relative non-existence and a counterpart to existence because it is a removal and negation. Notice why this might seem contradictory.
The objection is presenting a problem: how can something be both a division of non-existence and a counterpart to existence?
2
Examine the answer
Read Taftazani's answer. He says there is no impossibility. He distinguishes between two aspects: being 'not fixed' in terms of essence/concept versus being 'fixed' in terms of occurrence in the mind. Also distinguishes between being a division of non-existence (relative non-existence) and being a counterpart to existence (conceptually). Identify these distinctions.
Notice the Arabic phrases 'لَا ثَابِتًا' and 'ثَابِتًا' – they mark the distinction.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters. It shows how kalam handles abstract concepts and mental existence, and it prepares for later discussion on 'adam (non-existence). The answer uses a key principle: mental existence is different from external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلوق، لكونه عدماً مضافاً وقسيماً له. لكونه رفعاً له وسلباً . والجواب : أنه لا استحالة في كون المعنى لا ثابتاً من حيث الذات والمفهوم وثابتاً من حيث الحصول في العقل، ولا في كونه قسماً من العدم من حيث كونه عدماً مضافاً وقسيماً له من حيث المفهوم. وسيجيء لهذا زيادة تحقيق في بحث العدم .
Al-matluq (the absolute negation), because it is a non-existence that is relative (muḍāf) and a counterpart (qasīm) to it (i.e., existence). Because it is a removal (rafʿ) and negation (salb) of it. The answer: There is no impossibility in the meaning being not fixed (lā thābit) in terms of essence (dhāt) and concept (mafhūm) and fixed (thābit) in terms of occurrence in the mind, nor in it being a division (qism) of non-existence (ʿadam) in terms of being a relative non-existence and a counterpart to it in terms of concept. There will come for this further verification in the discussion of non-existence.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection defines 'al-matluq' (the absolute negation) as a relative non-existence (ʿadam muḍāf) and a counterpart to existence, because it is a removal and negation of existence. The answer states that there is no contradiction: the meaning (the negation) can be 'not fixed' in terms of its essence and concept (i.e., it has no external realization) while being 'fixed' in terms of its occurrence in the mind (as a mental concept). Similarly, it can be a division of non-existence in terms of being a relative non-existence, while still being a counterpart to existence in terms of concept (i.e., in the mind, absence is the counterpart of presence). The author notes that this will be further verified later in the discussion of non-existence.

Correction: The answer shows they are different aspects: it is a division of non-existence in terms of being relative, and a counterpart to existence in terms of concept (in the mind).

Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction by distinguishing between the status of the meaning in terms of essence/concept (non-fixed) and its occurrence in the mind (fixed), showing that mental existence permits conceptual counterparts.

Objection on Existence as Predicate and Its Refutation
Taftazani refutes an objection about taking existence as predicate in a disjunctive proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that arises when existence is taken as a predicate in a disjunctive proposition and how Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between conceptual difference and real identity.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction does not imply real separation; subject and predicate can differ in meaning yet refer to the same entity.

Taftazani addresses a subtle objection about the logical structure of disjunctive propositions involving existence. The objection claims that taking existence as a predicate leads to contradictions or infinite regress, but the answer clarifies the relationship between concept and reality, showing that no logical flaw follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the full objection and answer come through before breaking it down.
Pay attention to the two cases presented in the objection.
2
Understand the two horns of the objection
Identify the two scenarios: (1) existence is identical to quiddity, leading to superfluous affirmation and contradictory negation; (2) existence is different from quiddity, leading to subsistence of existence in non-existents and infinite regress. Note the additional points about self-identity and negation.
The objection uses logical reasoning to force a dilemma.
3
Grasp the answer's key distinction
The answer states that two things can be distinct in concept (mafhūm) but identical in identity (huwiyya). The body and the existent are the same entity (identical in re) even though the concepts differ. Also, absolute negation can be mentally fixed without entailing real contradiction.
This distinction resolves the apparent paradoxes.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this logical discussion applies to theological discourse about existence and attributes. Understanding the difference between conceptual and real identity prevents many philosophical errors.
This lesson connects to later discussions in kalam about existence and quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الوجود إن أخذ في هذه القضية المنفصلة محمولاً بمعنى أن الجسم إما كائن أو ليس بكائن، فإما أن يكون وجود الشيء نفس ماهيته فيلزم كون الجزء الإيجابي لغواً، مع أنه مفيد قطعاً، وكون الجزء السلبي تناقضاً لأن إطلاق السلب يناقض دوام الإيجاب، وإما أن يكون غيرها فيلزم في الإيجاب قيام الوجود بما ليس بموجود إن أخذ الموضوع خالياً عن الوجود، وتسلسل الموجودات إن أخذ موجوداً. وسيجيء بيانه، وجوابه في بحث الوجود، وأيضاً يلزم كون الشيء غيره وفيه اتحاد الاثنين، ويلزم في السلب تعقل النفي المستلزم لثبوته، وخلو الماهية عن الوجود المستلزم لقيام الوجود بالمعدوم عند ثبوته لها . والجواب : أنه لا امتناع في كون الشيئين متغايرين باعتبار، متحدين باعتبار، على ما تقرر من أن بين الموضوع والمحمول تغايراً بحسب المفهوم، واتحاداً بحسب الهوية . والمعنى: أن ما يقال له الجسم هو بعينه يقال له الموجود وكذا لا امتناع في كون النفي المطلق ثابتاً من حيث الحصول في العقل، ولا في قيام الوجود بما لم يكن موجوداً على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى، هذا كله إذا أخذ الوجود محمولاً.
And among them (the objections) is that if existence is taken as a predicate in this disjunctive proposition, meaning that the body is either existent (kā'in) or not existent (laysa bi kā'in), then either the existence of a thing is the same as its quiddity (māhiyya), in which case the affirmative part becomes superfluous (laghwan)—although it is certainly beneficial—and the negative part becomes contradictory (tanāquḍan) because the absolute negation contradicts the permanence of affirmation; or it is other than it, in which case in the affirmative part there follows the subsistence (qiyām) of existence in what is not existent if the subject is taken devoid of existence, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) of existents if it is taken as existent. Its explanation and answer will come in the discussion of existence. Also, it follows that a thing is other than itself and in it there is the union of two things; and in the negative part it follows the conception of negation that entails its affirmation, and the emptiness of the quiddity from existence that entails the subsistence of existence in the non-existent when it is established for it. The answer: There is no impossibility in two things being distinct (mutaghāyirayn) in one respect and identical (muttahidayn) in another respect, as established—that between the subject and predicate there is a distinction in terms of concept (mafhūm) and a unity in terms of identity (huwiyya). The meaning: that what is called the body is the very same that is called the existent. Similarly, there is no impossibility in the absolute negation being fixed in terms of occurrence in the mind, nor in the subsistence of existence in what was not existent, as will come, God willing. All this is when existence is taken as a predicate.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about taking existence as a predicate in a disjunctive proposition. The objection states that if existence is the same as the quiddity, then the affirmative part is redundant and the negative part is contradictory; if different, then problems of subsistence of existence in non-existents and infinite regress arise. Also, it leads to a thing being other than itself and union of two things, and negation entailing its affirmation. The answer explains that there is no impossibility: two things can be distinct in concept but identical in re: the body and the existent are conceptually different but refer to the same entity. Similarly, absolute negation can be mentally fixed without contradicting external reality, and existence can subsist in what was previously non-existent. The author notes further details will come in the discussion of existence.

Correction: The answer clarifies that they can be distinct in concept (mafhūm) but identical in identity (huwiyya); e.g., 'body' and 'existent' are conceptually different but refer to the same thing.

Existence as a predicate does not cause logical problems because subject and predicate can be distinct in concept but identical in reality.

Defending Existence as a Copula Against Regress
Taftazani answers an objection about taking existence as a copula leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends the use of existence as a copula against the objection of infinite regress of attributions by distinguishing mental judgments from external reality.

Core Idea
Mental relations need not mirror external ones to be true.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the logical role of existence as a copula in predications like 'the body is black.' The objection claims it leads to an infinite regress of attributions. Taftazani's response is a masterclass in distinguishing mental judgments from external reality, and it clarifies how relations can be true in the mind without corresponding to external entities. Follow closely to see how he dismantles the regress argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that taking existence as a copula leads to regress because the subject acquires an existential attribute (mawṣūfiyya), its opposite is privative, and the subject necessarily has it, leading to infinite regress. Also note the dismissal of 'mental considerations' as insufficient rebuttal.
The objection argues that attribution is a relation subsisting in the relata, not in the mind, and mental judgments must correspond to external reality.
2
Identify the three parts of the answer
Taftazani gives three responses: (a) the form of negation (like lā-mawṣūfiyya) may not be privative; (b) even if it were, the opposite of a privative is not necessarily existential; (c) mental judgments are not true by correspondence to the external, and relations occur only in the mind without contradiction.
The third point is the most important: external things cause those mental relations when apprehended.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this answer defends existence as a copula without falling into regress. The key is that mental relations do not require external correspondence; they are true in virtue of the dispositions of external things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا أخذ رابطة بأن يقال : الجسم إما أن يكون أسود أو لا يكون أسود، فيلزم في الجزء الإيجابي اتحاد الاثنين وقد سبق بجوابه، وأيضاً لما كان المحمول هنا وضعاً كان للموضوع موصوفية به وهي وجودية لأن نقيضها اللاموصوفية وهي عدمية، ويتصف بها الجسم ضرورة فيتسلسل حكم الموصوفيات ولا تندفع بكونها من الاعتبارات العقلية، لأن الموصوفية نسبة فتقوم بالمنتسبين لا بالعقل ولأن حكم العقل إن لم يطابق الخارج كان جهلاً، فإذا بطل الإيجاب تعين أن يكون الصادق دائماً هو الجزء السلبي، وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك بل تجوزون صدق الإيجاب في الجملة. والجواب : ما سيجيء من أن صورة السلب كاللا موصوفية لا يلزم أن تكون عدمية، ولو سلم فنقيض العدمي لا يلزم أن يكون وجودياً وأن الأحكام الذهنية لا يكون صدقها باعتبار المطابقة لما في الخارج وحصول النسب والإضافات في العقل فقط، لا ينافي انتسابها إلى الأمور الخارجية لأن معناه أن تكون تلك الأمور بحيث إذا عقلها عاقل حصلت في عقله تلك النسب والإضافات.
And if it (existence) is taken as a copula (rābiṭa), such that it is said: the body is either black or not black, then in the affirmative part there follows the union of the two (subject and predicate), and its answer has already preceded. Also, since the predicate here is a disposition (waḍʿ), the subject has an attribute (mawṣūfiyya) by it, which is existential (wujūdiyya) because its opposite (the non-attribution, lā-mawṣūfiyya) is privative (ʿadamiyya), and the body is necessarily described by it, so there is an infinite regress of the judgments of attributions (taṣalsul ḥukm al-mawṣūfiyyāt), and it is not repelled by their being mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya), because the attribution (mawṣūfiyya) is a relation (nisba), so it subsists in the two relata (al-muntasibayn), not in the intellect, and because the judgment of the intellect, if it does not correspond to the external, is ignorance (jahl). Thus, if the affirmative (part) is invalid, then it is determined that the true part is always the negative part, and you do not say that, but rather you allow the truth of the affirmative in general. The answer: What will come—that the form of negation (ṣūrat al-salb) like non-attribution (lā-mawṣūfiyya) is not necessarily privative; and if it is granted, then the opposite of a privative is not necessarily existential; and that mental judgments (al-aḥkām al-dhihniyya) are not true by virtue of correspondence to what is in the external; and the occurrence of relations (nisab) and additions (iḍāfāt) in the mind only does not contradict their attribution to external things, because its meaning is that those things are such that when an intellect apprehends them, those relations and additions occur in his mind.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about taking existence as a copula. The objection argues that if existence is a copula (e.g., 'the body is black'), then the subject has an attribute (mawṣūfiyya) which is existential; its opposite (non-attribution) is privative. The subject necessarily has that attribute, leading to infinite regress of attributions. This cannot be avoided by calling them mental considerations because attribution is a relation subsisting in the relata, not in the mind, and the intellect's judgment must match external reality, otherwise it is ignorance. Thus the negative part must always be true, but the opponent allows affirmative statements. The answer explains that the form of negation (like non-attribution) need not be privative; even if it were, the opposite of a privative is not necessarily existential. Moreover, mental judgments are not true by correspondence to the external; relations and additions occur only in the mind, but that does not prevent their attribution to external things, because the meaning is that the external things are such that when apprehended, those relations occur in the mind.

Key Terms
الرابطة
الموصوفية
اللاموصوفية
التسلسل
الاعتبارات العقلية
النسبة
الحكم الذهني
المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental judgments must correspond to external reality to be true.
Correction: The answer explains that mental judgments (like relations) are not true by correspondence to the external; they are true in the sense that external things cause those relations in the mind when apprehended.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the occurrence of relations in the mind not contradict their attribution to external things?
A) Because relations do not exist in the mind
B) Because external things themselves have relations
C) Because the meaning is that external things cause those relations when apprehended
D) Because relations are purely mental fictions

Key Takeaway
Taftazani defends existence as a copula by showing that mental relations do not require external correspondence, thus avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about something unseen (like the nature of Allah's attributes), do you ever feel pressured to prove it corresponds perfectly to some external reality? How does Taftazani's claim that mental judgments need not correspond externally free you from that pressure—or challenge you to think differently about certainty?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a logical argument because it seemed to lead to an infinite regress. After studying this lesson, are there regress arguments you should revisit, being open to the possibility that mental distinctions can stop the regress? What might that mean for your intellectual humility?
?
The objection in this passage assumes that if a relation is only mental, it is less real. Do you treat your inner spiritual experiences (like love for Allah, reliance on Him) as less real because they cannot be externally verified? How might Taftazani's answer reassure you or push you to take those mental realities more seriously?

Objection on the Absence of an Intermediary Between Existence and Non-Existence
Taftazani records an objection denying an intermediary between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological objection that there is no intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence, and how the author frames it as an objection to be answered later.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence are exhaustive; no third state is rationally conceivable.

In this passage, Taftazani introduces a key objection from opponents who deny any intermediary state between existence and non-existence. They argue that these two categories are mutually exclusive and cover all possibilities. The author acknowledges the objection but defers its full answer to a later discussion, while noting that some scholars affirm intermediaries and their consensus may carry weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author presents an objection ('we do not accept the absence of an intermediary') and then explicitly says its answer will come later.
This is a report of an opponent's view, not the author's own position.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'wāsiṭa' (intermediary), 'kawn' (existence), and 'lā kawn' (non-existence). The objection claims no intermediate state exists between being and non-being.
These terms are central to kalam discussions on the nature of things.
3
Analyze the citation from al-Mawāqif
Consider the reference to al-Mawāqif, which notes that scholars who affirm intermediaries are numerous enough that their agreement could serve as a proof (ḥujja). Reflect on what this implies about the strength of the objection.
The phrase 'قد يكون حجة' suggests the objection itself might be a proof, but the meaning is ambiguous without context.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Realize that this objection is part of a larger debate about the rational categories of existence. The answer, which will come later, explains why an intermediary is inconceivable between existence and non-existence.
This foreshadows a key doctrine in kalam: the rejection of a middle state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنا لا نسلم عدم الواسطة بين الوجود أو العدم وسيجيء بجوابه على أنها لا تعقل بين الكون واللاكون وما ذكر في المواقف من أن القائلين بها بلغوا في الكثرة حداً تقوم الحجة بقولهم معناه : أنه قد يكون حجة.
And among them (the objections) is that we do not accept the absence of an intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence or non-existence, and its answer will come later, that it is not conceivable between existence and non-existence (al-kawn wa al-lā kawn). And what was mentioned in al-Mawāqif—that those who affirm them (the intermediaries) reached such a number that proof (ḥujja) is established by their statement, its meaning: that it may be a proof.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an objection: the opponent denies that there is any intermediary between existence and non-existence (i.e., no third state). The objection implicitly argues that existence and non-existence are exhaustive and mutually exclusive. The author says the answer will come later: that an intermediary is inconceivable between existence and non-existence. Then he cites a reference to al-Mawāqif, noting that some scholars who affirm intermediaries (states like possibility or mental existence) are so numerous that their consensus can be considered a proof (ḥujja). The passage is incomplete, but it indicates that the objection will be fully answered elsewhere.

Key Terms
الواسطة
الكون
اللاكون
المواقف
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author himself claims there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The author is reporting an objection from opponents, not asserting his own position. He explicitly states that the answer will come later.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the claim that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence?
A) He accepts it without question
B) He rejects it emphatically
C) He reports it as an objection and says its answer will come later
D) He says it is a matter of consensus

Key Takeaway
The objection that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence is reported but its full answer is deferred, underscoring the importance of rational categories in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life are you trying to maintain a middle ground between truth and falsehood, knowing deep down that no such intermediary exists? What would it cost you to accept the dichotomy and act on it?
?
The objection denies that an intermediary is absent—meaning people want a third option. What is a situation where you have clung to ambiguity to avoid the clarity that would demand a difficult change? Are you willing to give that up?

The Three Schools of Sophists and Their Refutation
Classification of Sophists and proper response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three schools of Sophists as classified by Sanusi and why rational debate with them is impossible, requiring a different approach.

Core Idea
Sophists deny all knowledge; the only proof they accept is fire.

The challenge of skepticism is not new. In every age, some thinkers have denied the very possibility of certainty. The classical scholars classified such skeptics into three distinct groups and devised a unique method to refute them—one that bypasses argument altogether. This lesson explores that classification and the reasoning behind the dramatic response.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once through. Notice the three named groups and the author's conclusion about dealing with them.
The passage uses technical terms like 'La-adriyya', 'Inadiyya', and 'Indiyya.
2
Identify the three groups
Find the names of the three Sophist sects. For each, note their core claim: the La-adriyya doubt everything, including their own doubt; the 'Inadiyya claim every proposition has an equally strong counter; the 'Indiyya make truth relative.
The La-adriyya are considered 'better' by the author.
3
Understand why debate is futile
The author argues that Sophists deny all foundational knowledge (sense and self-evident truths). Since debate relies on shared premises, no common ground exists. Trying to prove these to them would strengthen their position.
This is the key reason for the drastic method.
4
Examine the prescribed method
The solution is to force them to admit sensory experience through pain (e.g., fire). Either they acknowledge pain (a sensed truth) and thus abandon their doctrine, or they persist and are eliminated.
This method is based on the necessity of sensory knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك عند الإخبار عن محسوس، ففي المعقول يكون شبهة لا أقل . قال : (ومنهم من قدح فيهما جميعاً (۱) وأمثلهم اللاأدرية القائلون بأني شاك وشاك في أني شاك (۲) وتمسكوا بشبه الفريقين ليورث شكاً ، والحق يعذبهم ولو بالنار ، ليعترفوا فتلغى ملتهم، أو يحرقوا فتنطفىء شعلتهم). أقول: أي في الحسيات والبديهيات جميعاً وهم السوفسطائية (۳) قال : في تلخيص المحصل، إن قوماً من الناس يظنون أن السوفسطائية قوم لهم نحلة ومذهب، ويتشعبون إلى ثلاث طوائف : اللاأدرية : وهم الذين قالوا نحن شاكون وشاكون في أنا شاكون، وهلم جرا . والعنادية : وهم الذين يقولون : ما من قضية بديهية أو نظرية إلا ولها معارضة ومقاومة، مثلها في القبول . والعندية : وهم الذين يقولون مذهب كل قوم حق بالقياس إليهم، وباطل (٤) بالقياس إلى خصومهم، وقد يكون طرفا النقيض حقاً بالقياس إلى شخصين، وليس في نفس الأمر شيء بحق . والمحققون على أن السفسطة مشتقة من سوماسطا (ه) ومعناه : علم الغلط والحكمة المموهة لأن سوفا اسم للعلم ووسطا للغلط ولا يمكن (٦) أن يكون في العالم قوم ينتحلون هذا المذهب بل كل غالط سوفسطائي في موضع غلطه ، ثم لا يخفى ما في كلام العنادية، والعندية من التناقض، حيث اعترفوا بحقية (۱) إثبات أو نفي سيما إذا تمسكوا فيما ادعوا بشبهة بخلاف اللاأدرية فإنهم أصروا (۲) على التردد والشك(٣) في كل ما يلتفت إليه، حتى في كونهم شاكين وتمسكوا بأنه لا وثوق على حكم الحس والعقل لما مر من شبه الفريقين ولا على الاستدلال لكونه فرعها، فلم يبق الا طريق التوقف، وغرضهم من هذا التمسك حصول الشك والتهمة لا إثبات أمر أو نفيه فلهذا كانوا أمثل طريقة من العنادية والعندية والمحققون على أنه لا سبيل إلى البحث والمناظرة معهم، لأنها لإفادة المجهول بالمعلوم ، وهم لا يعترفون بمعلوم أضلاً، بل يصرون على إنكار الضروريات أيضاً حتى الحسيات والبديهيات، وفي الاشتغال بإثباتهما التزام لمذهبهم، وتحصيل لغرضهم من كون الحسيات والبديهيات غير حاصلة بالضرورة بل مفتقرة إلى الاكتساب إذ عندنا لا يتصور كون الضروري مجهولاً يستفاد بالمعلوم، فالطريق معهم التعذيب ولو بالنار فإما أن يعترفوا بالألم وهو من الحسيات، وبالفرق بينه وبين اللذة وهو من العقليات وفيه بطلان لمذهبهم، وانتفاء لملتهم، وإما أن يصروا على الإنكار فيحترقوا وفيه اضمحلال لثائرة (٤) فتنتهم وانطفاء (ه) لثائر شعلتهم .
That is when reporting about a sensed thing; in the intelligible it becomes a doubt, no less. He said: 'And among them are those who attacked both (i.e., sense and intellect) entirely. The best of them are the La-adriyya (Agnostics), who say: I am doubtful, and doubtful that I am doubtful (2). They clung to the ambiguities of the two groups to produce doubt. The truth is to torture them, even with fire, so that they admit, thus their sect becomes invalid, or they burn, thus their spark is extinguished.' I say: i.e., in the sensed and the self-evident altogether, and they are the Sophists (3). He said in Talkhis al-Muhassal: Some people think that the Sophists are a people with a creed and a doctrine, and they branch into three sects: The La-adriyya: they are those who said we are doubtful and doubtful that we are doubtful, etc. And the 'Inadiyya (Obstinate): they are those who say: There is no self-evident or theoretical proposition except that it has an opposing and resisting counterpart equal in acceptance. And the 'Indiyya (Relativists): they are those who say: The doctrine of each people is true relative to them, and false relative to their opponents; the two opposites may both be true relative to two persons, and nothing is true in itself. The investigators hold that sophistry (safsata) is derived from 'sumasta' (5), meaning the science of error and disguised wisdom, because 'sufa' is a name for knowledge and 'usta' for error. And it is not possible that there exist in the world people who adopt this doctrine; rather, every errant person is a sophist in the place of his error. Then it is not hidden what is in the speech of the 'Inadiyya and the 'Indiyya of contradiction, since they admitted the truth of affirmation or negation, especially when they cling in what they claim to an ambiguity. Unlike the La-adriyya, for they insisted on hesitation and doubt in everything that is considered, even in their being doubtful. And they clung to the idea that there is no reliance on the judgment of sense and intellect due to what was mentioned of the ambiguities of the two groups, nor on reasoning because it is a branch of them. So nothing remains but the path of suspension. Their purpose in this clinging is to produce doubt and suspicion, not to affirm or negate anything. Hence they are better in method than the 'Inadiyya and the 'Indiyya. The investigators hold that there is no way to research and debate with them, because debate is to inform the unknown from the known, and they do not acknowledge any known at all; rather, they insist on denying the necessary truths as well, even the sensed and the self-evident. Engaging in proving these [sensed and self-evident] would be an endorsement of their doctrine and an achievement of their goal, namely that the sensed and self-evident are not obtained necessarily but require acquisition. For with us, it is inconceivable that the necessary is unknown and is learned from the known. So the way with them is torture, even with fire. Either they admit pain, which is among the sensed, and the distinction between it and pleasure, which is among the intelligibles—and in this is the nullification of their doctrine and the dissolution of their sect—or they persist in denial and burn, and in this is the extinction of the spark of their sedition and the quenching of the flame of their fire.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the Sophists (al-sufista'iyya) and how to refute them. It begins by quoting from the original text (likely al-Maqasid) that some denied both sense perception and intellect, and among them the best are the La-adriyya (Agnostics) who claim to be in doubt even about their own doubt. The author comments that these are the Sophists. He then cites a passage from Talkhis al-Muhassal explaining that people mistakenly think the Sophists are a coherent sect, but they actually branch into three groups: (1) La-adriyya: those who are in perpetual doubt, even about their doubt; (2) 'Inadiyya (Obstinate): those who claim that every proposition has an equally convincing counter-proposition; (3) 'Indiyya (Relativists): those who hold truth to be relative to each group, with no absolute truth. The author then notes the linguistic root of 'safsata' as derived from a Greek term meaning 'science of error.' He points out the contradiction in the 'Inadiyya and 'Indiyya positions, since they implicitly affirm truth in their arguments. The La-adriyya are less contradictory because they simply suspend judgment without affirming anything. However, the author concludes that debate with any Sophist is futile because they deny all foundations of knowledge (sense and self-evident truths). Trying to prove these to them would only support their claim that these require proof. Therefore, the only way is to force them through direct experience, such as burning them, so that they either admit to the reality of pain (a sensed truth) or persist and perish, extinguishing their influence.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية
اللاأدرية
العنادية
العندية
السفسطة
الحسيات
البديهيات
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Sophists are a single coherent sect.
Correction: The author shows they split into three distinct groups with different positions: La-adriyya (agnostics), 'Inadiyya (obstinate), and 'Indiyya (relativists).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is debate with the Sophists futile?
A) Because they are too intelligent for rational argument.
B) Because they deny all knowledge, including sense perception and self-evident truths, so there is no common ground.
C) Because they refuse to engage in any discussion.
D) Because they are an insignificant minority.

Key Takeaway
The only effective refutation of absolute skepticism is to confront skeptics with undeniable sensory experience, as rational debate is impossible when all premises are denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself doubting something so fundamentally that you questioned your own ability to know anything? What does this lesson tell you about the limits of such doubt and the role of undeniable experiences like pain?
?
The passage says the only effective response to absolute skepticism is to force the skeptic to confront undeniable reality—even if it takes fire. Are you willing to confront your own comfortable doubts with the same uncompromising truth, even when it burns?
?
When someone dismisses clear evidence in your life (e.g., a proven fact or a moral truth), do you engage in endless debate, or do you recognize the futility and instead point them to what is undeniably real—perhaps at the cost of discomfort? What would that look like today?
